\fs42\fsmilli21333 Doubling Down

\fs32 \cf3 Of the fifty or so patrons standing by the craps table, the only men who stared at tthe $10,000 in $500 and $1000 gambling chips that the enticing Rosette had laid down were the eunuchs. A sign noting \'93Reserved for Private Party\'94 sat on the table. Rosette wore an alluringly clinging orange chiffon dress, which coordinated nicely with her orange-colored $1000 \'93pumpkin\'94 chips. Maybe those were real pink pearls that dangled from her necklace. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The ca-chinking of slot machines competed with live music, multiple conversations, and the nearby roulette croupier call of \'93Rien ne va plus.\'94 With their senses of sight and hearing overloaded with these multiple inputs, the sensory-inebriated crowd at the craps table laughed and yelled encouragement as Rosette rolled the dice and implored the gods of the craps table. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Bring it home to your sweet peach pie momma. Momma needs a few new pairs of shoes!\'94 Rosette apparently liked posh footwear. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 My dad was seated about thirty yards away, the only player at a blackjack table. Large stacks of $25 chips lay on the table in front of him. He was play- ing three hands at the same time, and next to each were $100 in $25 chips. A King of Spades and ten of Hearts, a Queen of Clubs and a Queen of Diamonds, and a Jack of Clubs and a ten of Clubs, each hand with a value of 20, good blackjack hands, stared upward at the ceiling. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The dealer seemed mildly interested. She had seen it all, no doubt, but maybe not quite this. Her frilly white blouse obscured some wrinkled neck 
\fs24 \

\fs24 Never Split Tens! 
\f1\b0 \

\fs32 \cf3 skin, the indication of either too much weight or too much sun. \'93Betty Lou\'94 read her I.D. name tag. Her up-card, a four of Clubs, must have felt out of place in the presence of all that royalty. Her down card. .. Well, they\'92d soon all find out. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 My dad, Edward Oakley Thorp, sat in the chair by the stacks of chips. The twenty or so people watching were hushed. The noise was as loud as a Tibetan monk\'92s breathing while in the lotus position at sunrise. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Betty Lou held the deck of cards in her left hand. Oakley \'96 my sisters and I usually called him by his middle name, his father\'92s first name \'96 stared at the Jack of Clubs and ten of Clubs. He deliberately slid the ten of Clubs to the right of the Jack of Clubs and took four $25 chips from one of the stacks of chips and placed them next to the ten of Clubs. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Split,\'94 he announced to Betty Lou. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The crowd gasped. Dad now had four hands on the table. Was this the stupidest man they had ever seen or the cleverest? Betty Lou dealt a ten of Diamonds on top of the Jack of Clubs and a nine of Hearts on top of the ten of Clubs. He now had four pat hands. Maybe he was the cleverest man. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Betty Lou stared at Oakley. Maybe she was daring him to split again. Maybe she didn\'92t care. I\'92d bet on the latter. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley waved his hand over the cards, indicating he was standing pat with each of his four hands. No more splitting. Betty Lou flipped over her down card or, as gamblers refer to it, the \'93hole card,\'94 to reveal a nine of Hearts, for a total of 13 if you\'92re counting along. She dealt herself a Jack of Spades. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Dealer busts,\'94 she announced, although the applause of the crowd extolling the David-like victory of their hero signaled the redundancy of such words. She took sixteen $25 chips from her faux-gilded tray of chips and placed four next to each of Oakley\'92s winning bets. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It\'92s customary to tip the dealer for continued good luck from the blackjack gods,\'94 she advised. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93See, that\'92s the problem. I\'92m an atheist,\'94 Oakley countered. It was clear already that he and Rosette, assuming they would somehow meet, would never attain a satisfying spiritual connection. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 My dad was never really a nerdy math type of guy. He looked like a nerdy math type of guy, but surviving as a young boy in a blue-collar neighborhood on the northwest side of Chicago meant you had to be, if not tough, at least a smart-aleck. Oakley was definitely a smart-aleck. At this time he was in his late 20s. He was tall and thin, not athletic or muscled. If he\'92d been an athlete they would have called him \'93lanky.\'94 That appearance and the ever-present black-rimmed glasses fought a continual battle with his personality, the smart- aleck personality. If he had been born in, say, New York, Brooklyn, the Bronx, 
\fs24 \

\fs32 any of those areas, no doubt he would have become one of the world\'92s biggest nerds, just like my college roommate. But he wasn\'92t, and I was proud of him. All of us were, me, my sisters, and my mom. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Raking in his winnings, Oakley barely noticed a casino manager sitting down next to him. His red tie hung loosely at the neck, lying over a white-on- white embroidered shirt, the logo \'93Harrahs\'94 embroidered onto it. He didn\'92t wear a name tag. The buxom woman accompanying him could have been Rosette\'92s sister. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s a nice stack of chips,\'94 the manager observed. Oakley stared at the woman.\uc0\u8232 \'93You should know,\'94 he said, being, yes, the smart-aleck. \'93Splitting tens. Beginner\'92s luck, I guess, huh?\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Right.\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93Tsk, tsk. Don\'92t you know you don\'92t never split tens?\'94\u8232 \'93Oh, you know, I forgot.\'94\u8232 Sticking his face into Oakley\'92s, the manager tilted his head. \'93I seem to 
\fs24 \

\fs32 reco\'92nize your face. You were on t.v. for somethin\'92 or somethin\'92, right?\'94 \'93Splitting tens.\'94\uc0\u8232 I think Oakley should have quit while he was ahead. The manager without 
\fs24 \

\fs32 a name went to scratch his nose and knocked the pile of chips in front of Oakley to the floor. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, I\'92m so sorry. Please do allow me.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley bent over to pick up the chips, and the manager dove beneath the chairs with him. He elbowed Oakley in the kidney. You could still hear the clamor of excitement coming from Rosette\'92s craps table over Oakley\'92s restrained groans. As in the wild, never let your predator know your level of distress. It\'92s what Oakley learned in the neighborhood. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Pulling himself up, Oakley found two muscular security guards standing by the table. These were the kind of miscreants who really, really, wanted to be cops, only their cognitive skills didn\'92t allow them to pass the exam. No prob- lem existed with their psychological makeup. Not especially needing the rein- forcements, the manager advised, \'93We don\'92t want your action here, pal.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, and I thought you were just trying to pick my pocket.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The security guards didn\'92t respond particularly affectionately to this wise crack. They grabbed Oakley by either arm and dragged him away from the table. Oakley struggled to keep his balance. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Your chips\'92ll be counted and the cash it\'92ll be waiting for you at the cage.\'94 \'93How thoughtful.\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93We don\'92t want you here. You got. .. body odor, right Jimmy?\'94\u8232 \'93Right,\'94 said one of the guards. \'93You offend my smelling bad.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 \'93Hey, Jimmy, be nice.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He continued offering his brotherly advice to Oakley as they walked to the cashier\'92s cage. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But, a lot of our patrons, they complain about it. Next time you walk into this establishment, my esteemed associates. .. well, I don\'92 know if I\'92ll be able to control \'91em, you know? Somebody might find you sometime lying face down somewhere on a cactus.\'94

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 Three Months Earlier CBS Television Studios New York City 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 On the right side of the stage of the television show \'93To Tell The Truth,\'94 as seen from the live studio audience, three, well, nerdy-looking men sat. They all wore black-rimmed glasses. They all had greenish, brownish tweed jackets over a white shirt and a black tie. They all looked like college pro- fessors. Maybe they all were college professors, but at the beginning of the show I could only be sure that Oakley was one. He was at MIT at the time. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The genial host, beloved by all Americans for his quiet, respectful wit, was Bud Collyer. He always seemed to have a chuckle in his voice, a result no doubt of truly enjoying his job. His desk sat to the left of the professor- contestants and faced that of the four celebrity panelists. I always loved that term, \'93celebrity panelists.\'94 Why not just \'93celebrities\'94 or \'93panelists\'94 or, for that matter, \'93guest stars\'94? \'93Celebrity panelists\'94 seemed to be a term embraced by all Americans. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Tom Poston, the comic actor, was one, sitting on the far left end of the panel as viewed by the contestants. Then there were Peggy Cass, the comic actress, Orson Bean, another comic actor, and, at the far right end, the glamorous Kitty Carlisle. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Kitty wore a dress that had a rigid vertical bodice, cantilevered out about six inches from her body. I\'92m sure her dress designer was also the costume designer for Ming the Merciless, as played by Charles Middleton in the clas- sic Flash Gordon series of Oakley\'92s boyhood. After the question and answer portion of the program was concluded and the panel voted, Oakley would shake hands with Ms. Carlisle first. Vivian, who was to become my mom, was there as well, mingling with the celebrities and Edward Thorp imper- sonators, her genuineness and always well-coiffured moderate-length blonde hair guaranteeing that she would be noticed among the celebrities. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Bud spoke to the camera. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s our distinguished celebrity panel, and now let\'92s meet our special guests.\'94 The nerdy men spoke in turn. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93My name is Edward Thorp.\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93My name is Edward Thorp.\'94\u8232 \'93My name is Edward Thorp.\'94\u8232 It was now time for Bud to provide the background information. Then the 
\fs24 \

\fs32 celebrity panelists would interrogate the three, trying to determine who was \'93the real Edward Thorp.\'94 At the end of the show, after the panelists voted, Bud would say, \'93Will the real Edward Thorp please stand up.\'94 Often, the guy who two or most of the panelists thought was the real special guest would start to rise from his seat, then sit own, and then the real surprise guest would stand up to audience applause. Sometimes they had two fake risings. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Sometimes, if I remember, the real guest would rise, then sit down, then rise again. It\'92s amazing how you can create so many combinations of risings and sittings among three people. A celebrity of sorts in his own right, the real surprise guest would answer a few questions from the celebrity panel. On this show, Oakley would rise and stay risen. Two of the four panelists would correctly guess he was the real Edward Thorp. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 First, Bud had to provide that aforementioned background. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Edward Thorp is a professor of mathematics. At the age of seven, he was calculating in his head the number of seconds in a year. While at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, he developed a technique called \'91card counting\'92 that has enabled him to win tens of thousands of dollars playing the game of blackjack in casinos from Las Vegas to Puerto Rico. His book on the subject, 
\f2\i Beat the Dealer
\f1\i0 , made 
\f2\i The New York Times 
\f1\i0 best-seller list. It is currently the most frequently requested volume in the Las Vegas Public Library.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 After this public library note, Oakley broke into a wide, obviously genuine grin, a beaming smile he possessed that could illuminate an entire room. It\'92s surprising that not all the panelists would guess, on that evidence alone, that he was the real Edward Thorp. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Peggy Cass, let\'92s start the questioning with you.\'94 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 April 16, 1955 Zellerbach Auditorium UC Berkeley Campus 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 A forty-foot-long cloth sign, blue on gold, draped over the entrance to Zellerbach Auditorium, read \'93The Pacific Coast Collegiate Jazz Festival \'96 Welcome to UC Berkeley.\'94 Oakley told me he loved playing at the Pacific Coast 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 1 Recollections 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 7 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 8 
\fs24 Never Split Tens!

\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Collegiate Jazz Festival, the PCCJF, at Berkeley. Zellerbach Auditorium stands on the west side of Lower Sproul Plaza. You walk down the stairs to the plaza, and it looms in front of you. Mr. Zellerbach must have been a heavy alumni donor. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 All the student demonstrations, okay, riots, during the Free Speech Movement of a later decade, would be held up the stairs, to the east, toward the Berkeley hills, on Upper Sproul Plaza, or simply, as it was usually called, Sproul Plaza. Because it faced the administration building, Sproul Plaza, with the leg- endary Ludwig\'92s Fountain, named after a famous frolicking dog, allowed fren- zied students to walk straight to the stairs of the administration building for their sit-ins or love-ins or whatever \'93in\'94 was on the activist menu that day. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Lower Sproul Plaza, on the other hand, was a football-field-sized area for throwing Frisbees and listening to the loosely structured bongo band on a Sunday afternoon. Oakley told me the interplay of rhythms was both mesmer- izing and relaxing. Completely unstructured, without a solo, there were up to a dozen men, each playing a given rhythm over and over. They played, literally, and I don\'92t mean figuratively, I mean literally, for hours. Once, a white guy with no idea of the protocol started playing a solo. Oakley told me the guy got corrected quickly. Nothing is quite like a bunch of guys with foot-high Afros skewering you visually \'96 figuratively, not literally. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 At intermissions of concerts, the audience would stream out onto the plaza. No problems with traffic or bus noise would greet them. They\'92d just look up at the sky and enjoy the stars and the Moon until the house lights would flash, announcing the end of intermission and the beginning of the second part of the concert. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was about 10:30 p.m. this Saturday night, and the winners of the com- petitions had been announced. The host band, from UC Berkeley, had invited the UCLA band to perform with them for the final concert. They both sat on the stage. The guest soloist, drummer Ed Shaughnessy from L.A., was playing with the host band from Berkeley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The music stands in front of the saxophone players from UCLA had \'93Bruin Big Band\'94 written on them in a fancy script. Maybe it was Peinaud. The music stands in front of the saxophone players from the Berkeley band simply had \'93UC Jazz\'94 written on them, in block letters. Maybe Helvetica. If you were a high school band member attending the concert you got the unsubtle mes- sage that UCLA values football over academics while Berkeley values academ- ics over football. I guess Zellerbach appreciated that. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The auditorium was jammed. Dave LeFebvre was directing the Bruin big band, and Dr. David W. Tucker was directing the UC jazz band. An audience of not only the college musicians performing at the festival but also Berkeley students and Bay Area jazz fans filled the auditorium. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley, at the time 22 years old, stood in front of the bands. He played sensuous trumpet riffs accompanying a woman vocalist. That was Vivian, who as I said was to become my mom. Oakley played a top-of-the-line trumpet, the Vincent Bach Stradivarius. Along with the Benge, it was the finest trum- pet of the era. If you, of course, had a lot of money, and were a symphony trumpet player or big-time jazz player, you could get Roland Schilke in Chicago to make one of his hand-made silver Schilke trumpets for you. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The Bach served Oakley well enough. It had a nice dark sound. I remember he complained that the E at the top of the staff was a little flat, so he had to use the alternative first and second valve fingering to stay in tune. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Vivian, in her early 20s, about five feet three inches tall, was slim, and wore an alluring but sophisticated cocktail dress. Vivian possessed the aura of a society gal, one of the 500, invited to the Cotillions, the coming-out parties. She always carried herself with respect. I still don\'92t know exactly why she went for a nerdy mathematician. I guess, no, I\'92m fairly sure, she loved his intellect. He kept her off-balance with his smart-aleck personality. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 I learned in my dating years that after a while a lot of women get tired of being wined and dined by the shallow types. One of my girlfriends, after we moved back to southern California for Oakley\'92s position at UC Irvine, told me why she was attracted to me. Deborah Elwood said to me, \'93Because you\'92re a good man.\'94 Mom, Vivian, must have felt the same way about Oakley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 She sang the tune \'93I\'92ve Never Been in Love Before,\'94 accompanied by Oakley playing licks and the joint bands: 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 I\'92ve never been in love before,\uc0\u8232 Now all at once it\'92s you,\u8232 It\'92s you forever more.\u8232 I\'92ve never been in love before,\u8232 I thought my heart was safe,\u8232 I thought I knew the score.\u8232 But this is wine that\'92s all too strange and strong. I\'92m full of foolish song 
\f1\i0\fs24 \

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 And out my song must pour.\uc0\u8232 So please forgive this helpless haze I\'92m in, I\'92ve never really been in love before.\'94 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 She stared at Oakley as she sang. Everyone in the hall must have gotten the feeling the look had more content than to ensure they were on the same musi- cal page. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Now it was Oakley\'92s turn. He played eighteen bars, the first two stanzas, and then Vivian came back with the chorus. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 When the tune was over, the audience applauded, as much for the music as for the admittedly syrupy sensuality, I\'92m sure. There were whistles and cheers, too. Oakley and Vivian continued staring at each other. I\'92d bet more than one person in the audience was thinking, \'93Get a room!\'94 respectable society girl or not. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Les, the emcee, walked onto the stage, applauding. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Let\'92s hear it for the Bruin Big Band from UCLA, under the direction of David LeFebvre, and trumpeter Ed Thorp.\'94 He pointed to them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah! And our own Wednesday Night Band from the University of California, Berkeley, under the direction of Dr. David W. Tucker. Yeah! Featuring Vivian, Vivian Artisen on vocals.\'94 Again, he pointed to them. \'93Let\'92s hear it! Come on!\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley and Vivian held hands, stared at each other, and smiled. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 *** 
\fs24 \

\fs32 If you drive east on Ashby Avenue southeast of the UC Berkeley campus, past the Claremont Hotel, you\'92d enter the tunnel that would take you under the Berkeley hills to the communities of Concord and Walnut Creek. There, sum- mer days often have temperatures in the 90s. Similarly, south of the Burlingame hills you\'92ll find the temperatures in the south Bay, Santa Clara, San Jose, and Palo Alto, to be considerably warmer than in San Francisco. The weather in San Francisco, Oakland, El Cerrito, Richmond, Hayward, Berkeley, and other towns within those hills is dominated by the cool temperatures of the bay. In the morning and evenings you frequently have to wear a light jacket. It was typically chilly this Sunday morning at 8:00 a.m. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The guitar player with the UCLA band sat on somebody\'92s trumpet case next to the bus waiting on Bancroft Avenue behind Eshleman Hall and alter- nately played riffs from \'93Joy Spring\'94 and blew on his fingers for warmth. A couple of band members helped the drummer carry his set from Zellerbach to the bus. They loaded it into the belly of the bus and then stood around listening to the guitar player before climbing into the bus for the ride back to a warm Los Angeles. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley walked to the bus carrying his trumpet case. Vivian held his free hand with one hand and carried a bottle with the other. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Here\'92s some orange juice for the ride. To replenish your energy.\'94 \'93Excellent! Something to remember you by. Well, it was nice.\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93Is that it, then? Just a one-night stand between starving student musicians?\'94 \'93I\'92m not starving.\'94 He sipped from the bottle. \'93Not now, anyway.\'94\u8232 \'93A one-night stand then it is.\'94\u8232 \'93Correct me if I\'92m wrong,\'94 said Oakley, \'93but Friday night and Saturday 
\fs24 \

\fs32 night. That makes it a two-night stand.\'94\uc0\u8232 Vivian unclasped her hand from Oakley\'92s and gestured as she sang a line 
\fs24 \

\fs32 from \'93These Foolish Things.\'94 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 You came, you saw, you conquered me. 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley took another sip. \'93Yes, a libation worthy of Edward the Conqueror. Except, I think you said I\'92m the greatest trumpet player ever. You conquered me.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93So this is it,\'94 said Vivian beginning to be resigned.\uc0\u8232 \'93Depends. What do you mean by \'91it\'92?\'94\u8232 \'93Just like a law student! If you weren\'92t such a good kisser.\'94\u8232 The other musicians had long ago climbed into the bus. The driver beeped 
\fs24 \

\fs32 for Oakley.\uc0\u8232 \'93Trained embouchure. Well, g\'92bye.\'94\u8232 \'93Edward?!\'94\u8232 \'93I gotta go home now and train my embouchure.\'94\u8232 \'93But!\'94\u8232 \'93Is seven o\'92clock okay with you?\'94\u8232 \'93What?\'94\u8232 \'93Seven o\'92clock. I\'92ll drive up in my red T-Bird next Friday. I\'92ll be by about 
\fs24 \

\fs32 seven o\'92clock. And just because you\'92re so nice, I\'92m taking you to Lake Tahoe.\'94 My father had to be the smart-aleck. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, yeah. I mean, yes. Yes. Yes, Edward. Oh, you. .. you. . ..\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The bus door closed and Oakley walked to a window seat. He and Vivian waved good-bye. He threw her a kiss. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, you!\'94 she cried out and kissed him back.

\f0\b\fs96 \cf2 2\uc0\u8232 
\fs42\fsmilli21333 A Shayna Madel 
\f1\b0\fs24 \cf3 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 \'93And for God\'92s sake, Julian, if she wants to let you kiss her, then kiss her,\'94 his mother advised. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93And if she wants to let you. ..\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93Marcel!\'94\u8232 \'93The boy is twenty-five, Anne.\'94\u8232 Most of the European Jews who settled in Chicago in the decades around 
\fs24 \

\fs32 the turn of the twentieth century lived in small apartments in the Maxwell Street area. After earning a little money they moved west to the Lawndale district, centered on Douglas Boulevard and its wide boulevard park. The elementary school was named Theodore Herzl, after the pioneering Zionist. Immigrants from each little town, or 
\f2\i shtetl
\f1\i0 , in Eastern Europe had formed their own orthodox synagogue along Douglas Boulevard. To this day, those synagogues remain, now converted to churches or community centers as the Jewish families have long sincenmoved. Jews with some wealth opted instead for the Hyde Park neighborhood, its focus being the University of Chicago. They would always consider themselves superior to the west side Jews. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 As the Lawndale district changed, the Jewish families there moved further west to the Austin neighborhood. The Braun family settled in a mod- est home on Long Avenue, one block north of Madison Street with its kosher bakeries, grocery stores, and butchers.

\fs32 \'93Trivial,\'94 judged Julian. \'93Just assign a probability to each of your oppo- nent\'92s possible moves and then a random number generator selects the move based on the pre-assigned skill level and the various probabilities.\'94

\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But then the sequence would be limited in length to the value of the modulus, right?\'94

\fs24 \

\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley had been advised by one or more professors as an undergraduate at Cornell that cramming for exams was deleterious to your performance. You\'92d do better if you just cleared your brain for an hour or even a half an hour before the exam, particularly the two- or three-hour-long final exams, if you just took a walk. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley wasn\'92t aware at that time of the two modes of processing of the brain. Working on an exam utilizes the left mode of processing, the sequential, analytical mode. This was true even if you\'92re writing an essay for an English exam. You still put the words on the paper one by one, in sequence. The right mode of brain processing, in contrast, is global, synthetic. I hate to use the word \'93creative,\'94 but creativity seems to flow from right-brain processing. If Oakley, while he was an undergraduate at Cornell University, had been aware of the split-brain research being done by Roger Sperry now thirty miles away from him at Cal Tech he would certainly have embraced regularly taking a stroll before exams. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 His senior year at Cornell, he gave it a shot on a professor\'92s recommenda- tion. He performed brilliantly on the exam and from then on he followed the same routine. His chosen route was from College Town, past the Law School and Engineering Quad, past Willard Straight Hall, and onto the Arts Quad. He told me that he did deviate it from it once, before a cultural anthropology class he took as an elective. He noted that so many facts had to be retained for each tribe, their tools, institutions, and religion, for a few, that cramming until the last minute seemed the only way to get an \'93A\'94 in the class. He slept for only one hour the night before the final exam, and aced it, to use the phrase in vogue at that time at Cornell. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 At UCLA he found a route that also worked. His path of choice was north on the southeast part of campus, parallel to Hilgard Avenue. Plenty of palm trees graced the campus, but this route passed by a pine grove and its curious squirrels. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley was walking with Professor Angus Taylor, age 43, and Allan Wilson, age 31, a mathematics post-doctoral student and Oakley\'92s best friend. Professor Taylor was to author a book in 1959 on calculus, 
\f2\i Calculus with Analytical Geometry
\f1\i0 , that was to become a standard in colleges for decades. He was Oakley\'92s Ph.D. advisor. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan shuffled a deck of cards with one hand. He often brought it with him to class if the class became boring. Allan himself was a bright fellow. All three carried notebooks as they strolled through campus from the Mathematical Sciences building to get an ice cream cone at the Terrace Food Court. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley was bothered by the lack of an attractive name for the building where he spent most of his time. Whereas many buildings on campus were named after donors, the buildings housing engineering, math, and the sci- ences had generic names. \'93Geology,\'94 \'93Physics and Astronomy Building,\'94 and \'93Mathematics Sciences Building\'94 were institutionally bland enough. You had to wonder, though, about the effect on the psychologies of engi- neering students whose studies were housed in, and I\'92m not making this up, \'93Engineering I,\'94 \'93Engineering IV,\'94 and \'93Engineering V.\'94 I don\'92t know what happened to II and III. Oakley would reminisce about the Ivy-covered names at his alma mater, Goldwin Smith Hall, Morrill Hall, and Lincoln Hall. Even the buildings on the Engineering Quad had names: Phillips Hall, Carpenter Hall, where the 
\f2\i Cornell Engineer 
\f1\i0 student-run magazine had its offices, Kimball Hall, and so on. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was a typical end of class tumult, with students and faculty everyone going from one class to another or to a library. The over-abundance of pul- chritude would distract a blind monk. You know, the altruistic type, those who would volunteer to lead seeing people across the street. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan stopped shuffling the deck to offer a suggestion to Oakley.\uc0\u8232 \'93You gotta tell her sooner or later, Ed.\'94\u8232 \'93What do the cards say?\'94 asked Oakley.\u8232 Allan knew that a standard deck of 52 cards couldn\'92t provide the answer. \'93Dr. Taylor, do you happen to have a deck of tarot cards? \fs24 \

\fs32 home,\'94 asked Allan.\uc0\u8232 \'93Ed, you should be proud of being a mathematician. It\'92s the language of 
\fs24 \

\fs32 science. Maybe she\'92ll get excited by it,\'94 said Professor Taylor. Oakley didn\'92t think it was the best advice. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah. Honey, wanna get a look at my hypotenuse? Can I bisect your acute angle with my linear operator?\'94 Professor Taylor, as all brilliant men, had an excellent sense of humor. You could joke with him and not feel awkward. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s funny. Why do mathematicians always have to prove themselves?\'94 Oakley wondered out loud. Professor Taylor loved it. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Now, that\'92s an interesting existential concept.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 They all laughed at the implications. Professor Taylor greatly enjoyed ban- tering with his students. They would soon become his colleagues, and very little compares with the camaraderie of bright academics gathered at holiday parties or in the evenings at their annual academic meetings. Truly bright people have a shine in their physiognomies, generated I think by their ultra- sensitivity to stimuli. It\'92s like a San Andreas fault earthquake. The first time you experience it, you don\'92t know what it is, but after being told, \'93Oh, did you feel the earthquake?\'94 you\'92ll be aware of it forever and can become the instructor for the newcomers who hadn\'92t yet experienced it. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 So it is with the faces of the intellectually gifted. You can recognize the shine in their faces, but until you\'92re told that the person is a brilliant astrono- mer or mathematician or book editor, you don\'92t know its origin, just what it is. Henceforth, you\'92re sensitized to, and aware of, the glow. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 I join all the others who think that \'93Barney\'92s\'94 is a stupid name for a casino. I mean, who is Barney? Sounds like a farmer to me. Maybe he was a farmer and then got tired of it and so he built a casino in some soybean field. No one names a casino after their first name. You have Harrah\'92s and the Riverside Hotel. Name it Cal Neva, to attract residents of both California and Nevada. Calling it Neva Cal would have sounded too much like a food supplement, so they chose Cal Neva. Casino restaurant food has a bad enough reputation. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Then you have all the gambling-sounding names and desert-sounding names. The Four Queens. The Golden Nugget. The Mint. The Horseshoe. The Sands. The Dunes. The Stardust. The Wagon Wheel. The Westward Ho is a motel, but it\'92s still a western sounding name. Yes, I know about Harold\'92s casino in Reno, but that is Reno. I just can\'92t understand naming a casino after Barney. I expect to find one named Billy Bob\'92s or Bubba\'92s. Maybe it\'92s the letter \'93B\'94 at fault. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 In any case, although they took rooms at the more luxurious Harrah\'92s, Barney\'92s in Stateline, Nevada, on the shores of South Lake Tahoe, is where Oakley, Vivian, Allan, and his girlfriend Joan decided to try out Baldwin\'92s system. The men who would later become known as the Four Horsemen of Aberdeen \'96 Roger Baldwin, Wilbert Cantey, Herbert Maisel, and James McDermott \'96 were mathematicians at the Aberdeen Proving Ground in Maryland and, using clunky electrical/mechanical calculators of the era, perhaps the Friden brand, they had laboriously devised a basic system for the game of blackjack. With the approval of their supervisor, Abe Karp, they had spent several thousand hours over a period of one and one-half years in 1952

\fs32 The green felt-covered blackjack table, with the name \'93Barney\'92s\'94 printed on it in red ink, included a silvery money tray containing rows of chips, colored according to their value and each emblazoned, of course, with the name \'93Barney\'92s.\'94 Next to it was a discarded pile of cards. \fs32 Oakley told me that he loved Vivian\'92s name. Not only did he love her first name, he loved her last name, Artisen. She was a creative person by any measure. \fs32 She and Oakley, the mathematician, would permute the letters of her name to see what they would spell. Then they\'92d come up with stories about the word or phrase. The game had started early in their dating, when Oakley had scraped up enough cash to take her to a Frank Sinatra appearance at the Hollywood Bowl. They realized that the letters of \'93Sinatra\'94 and \'93Artisen\'94 were identical except for switching her \'93e\'94 for one of his \'93a\'92s.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 With three vowels, and four of the most commonly appearing consonants in materials printed in the English alphabet \'96 \'93n,\'94 \'93r,\'94 \'93s,\'94 \'96 and \'93t\'94 many words would result from the permutations. \fs32 Before Oakley even thought about blackjack seriously, he knew intuitively that you don\'92t sleep where you gamble. An old expression says you \'93don\'92t ____ where you eat,\'94 meaning don\'92t perform any unseemly acts aimed at, or especially with, fellow employees if you want to keep your job. Professional gamblers, unless they are severely ego-deprived, know that the less the house knows about you the better. If you\'92re a big player and stay in their hotel, they\'92ll get your credit card number and check your credit, see if you\'92re a big spender, meaning in their terms, a high roller, determine if you like to have women in your room, and get a list of who you speak to on the phone. \fs32 \cf3 Joey and Phil were working the graveyard shift at Barney\'92s. Joey was at a table different from that of the previous day, but in the same pit. In South Lake Tahoe, some of the skiers/gamblers would gamble before the slopes opened. After sundown, unless you were skiing at a location with night lights, the ski- ers would go to their hotels or lodges, sit by the fire, have some hot cider or rum, and then take their shower. After dinner they\'92d hit the tables. Those were the skiers who would gamble for diversion. Those who hit the tables before the slopes opened were the gamblers who would also ski. The casinos are aware of the difference, and are accustomed to seeing full tables even before the graveyard shift was over, usually 7:00 a.m. It was now about 6:15 a.m. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Three players were at the table, along with Oakley. He sat to the right of Joey, in what is called \'93third base.\'94 That allowed him to see all the cards that were discarded by losing players before he had to make his playing strategy decisions. That should have been a tip-off to Phil that this was not the rube they believed they had met the previous day. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Judging from the piles of chips in front of them and the number of chips in their betting squares, none of the other players was a either a high roller or a big winner. Five $5 chips lay on the table in front of the player directly to Joey\'92s left, in \'93first base,\'94 and four $5 chips lay in front of the player to his left. Each had bet two $5 chips. Four $1 chips lay in the betting square of the player directly to Oakley\'92s right. He had no chips remaining other than those. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley, in contrast, was playing only $1 chips. Fourteen $1 chips lay on the table in front of him, and he had placed only two $1 chips in his betting square. If he was trying to deflect any suspicion, his playing only $1 chips and

\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley decided that the nuptials would be held in the Royal Garden Wedding Chapel. He and Allan had passed a number of chapels, and one can be sure that they all offered the same spiritually fulfilling, righteous, respectful, and, most importantly, statute-satisfying wedding service, suitable for all faiths, although they all would only be about ten minutes long. The cost would be about the same at any of them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 As soon as Oakley saw the name, however, he announced his choice. \'93Royal Garden\'94 didn\'92t appeal to him because of any pretensions toward monarchs and their accoutrements and fawning serfs, but rather because Royal Gardens Blues was the first tune played by the high school dance band in which he had first played jazz. He could still scat sing the tune, \'93dah, da da da da dot dot dot, de dah dah, da da da da dot dot dot.\'94 A simple little blues ditty; it was perfect for a high school dance band just starting out, and Oakley found that it remained in his brain, waiting for a reprise at any par- ticularly unoccupied moment. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The dance band was led by a bookish kid from the concert band who played the sax, and his concept of putting together a dance band for sock hops wasn\'92t to get any gigs but to put on a concert. Every week on a Sunday afternoon, the guys would get together at his house and rehearse a couple tunes. He only had a couple of arrangements, or charts, as jazz players call them. Royal Garden Blues was the first tune they\'92d rehearse every Sunday. They had it down pat, but the band never got any gigs. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley decided this nice bandleader was a lousy businessman, and so he formed his own band. He actually bought some charts and within weeks got booked not only for the sock hops in the basement of the First Baptist Church the evenings of Saturday afternoon football games but also a couple private parties and a wedding. Yes, The Deuces, composed of high school kids, got a booking for a wedding. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 The bass player in the band would become a music major in college and get hired by the New York Philharmonic, with whom he would gain renown for not only his musicianship but also for his bringing jazz to the composi- tion of orchestral pieces. \fs32 One thing about Salvation Army stores \'96 they have large storefront windows. As with other clothing stores, those windows are meant to attract shoppers. If anyone enters a Salvation Army store, however, they do so not as casual shoppers but with this destination in mind. No one window shopping for clothing actu- ally decides to enter a Salvation Army store. Vivian and Joan were the exception. After leaving the florist, they found the Salvation Army store around the corner.

\fs32 The trip to the tailor being partially successful, Oakley wore the white shirt, tuxedo, and bow tie. His pants legs were held up by safety pins. Stanley was very upset. \fs32 They had seen, no doubt, numerous spur-of-the-moment marriages, but Pastor Elmer, Alice, and the organist, Eleanor, had probably never seen the premature show of affection they were witnessing. \fs24 \

\fs32 Certainly, the pastor, Alice, and Eleanor had never seen the best man and maid of honor partake in the pulpit passion. Allan and Joan had joined Oakley and Vivian in their own extended embrace, as much out of passion as out of relief that the charade was over. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 As soon as Pastor Elmer had finished pronouncing the marriage of Mr. Thorp and Mrs. Thorp, Eleanor had begun to sing \'93The Anniversary Song,\'94 accompanied by herself at the organ. \fs24 \

\fs32 \'93. . . kiss the bride,\'94 Pastor Elmer finished directing Oakley, in one of the great anti-climactic statements in modern nuptial history.

\fs32 \'93Okay,\'94 said Oakley. \'93Let\'92s say I have a box with ten red balls and ten white balls. If I pull out a red ball I win and if I pull out a white ball I lose. \fs32 \cf3 Nine out of ten Americans don\'92t realize that the city of broad shoulders not only has a beach but also an extensive beach stretching twenty miles north and south along Lake Michigan. The master plan of city planner Daniel Burnham included yacht harbors, extensive parks, and a road parallel to the beach stretching from the far north side of Chicago to the far south side. Office and apartment buildings would not be allowed within nearly a mile from the Lake Front, as it was known. The drive along Lake Shore Drive appealed to bicy- clists, walkers, runners, as well as those in automobiles. Pedestrian overpasses allowed beachgoers to avoid the traffic. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 If you drove directly east into Chicago along Congress Parkway you would proceed under the massive post office building, into the Loop, past Michigan Avenue, and into Grant Park. Michigan Avenue compares to other grand boulevards of the worlds, with The Bund in Shanghai an appropriate refer- ence. Both Michigan Avenue and The Bund are associated with waterfronts. If you stood in Grant Park and looked westward, you\'92d see a stretch of buildings from north to south. The Conrad Hilton, the Chicago Symphony building, and others were situated there, straight, in a row, forming a backdrop for the park rather than an intrusion. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 At the end of Congress Parkway, where the road terminates with right- or left-hand turns onto Lake Shore Drive, was the answer of Chicago to the great fountains of Rome. Buckingham Fountain, designed by Burnham\'92s collabora- tor Edward Bennett, is a nearly three-hundred-foot-diameter structure, with water-spouting stone classical figures directing their streams into the moat surrounding them. On summer nights, light shows of multiple colors ema- nating from underwater lamps accompanied the water jets, in well-designed

\fs32 \'93John?\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93Nope,\'94 answered Professor Selfridge.\u8232 \'93I\'92ve got one more question,\'94 Professor Taylor said to Oakley.\u8232 Oakley thought he had done pretty well. A dissertation defense is fre- 
\fs24 \

\fs32 quently a formality. The faculty advisor, in Oakley\'92s case Angus Taylor, had guided Oakley\'92s work throughout and was aware that it was suitable for awarding the Ph.D. Sometimes, rarely, a particularly difficult additional com- mittee member would find a flaw in the proof, a lemma that required further substantiation, an assumption that was unwarranted, or some other problem. Such defects were usually not fatal. At worst, the candidate would be directed to provide additional steps in the proof. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Mathematicians are a rare breed. They are like classical pianists or chess masters. Either you have this ability to intuitively \'93see\'94 the logic or you don\'92t. Unlike other fields, such as astronomy, physics, biology, chemistry, botany, anthropology, sociology, political science, art history, architecture, and litera- ture, where a large body of previous knowledge must be studied, in mathe- matics you need only complete a proof. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 In the sciences, in particular, our minds are too feeble to deduce the count- less manifestations of the laws of nature. It was the astronomer Sir Arthur Eddington, after all, who supposedly had said that if Earth were enshrouded in clouds we would never have known that stars exist. You must first observe the manifestations of nature, and then try to understand them in terms of those laws of nature. Acquiring Ph.D. level proficiency in most subjects requires, in short, the assimilation of a large body of knowledge. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 In mathematics, in contrast, someone with the requisite intuitive logic skills can get a Ph.D. in mathematics in two or three years. State a proposi- tion, see the solution, and do the proof. Anyone who takes ten years to get a Ph.D. in mathematics doesn\'92t have the ability. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs26\fsmilli13333 65 
\f1\b0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 You have child prodigies in piano and chess for similar reasons. You either have the music in your head or have an advanced mode of both right and left brain processing that allows you to see dozens of moves ahead, respectively, in which cases you attain a professional level quickly, or you don\'92t. One never hears of a child prodigy in astronomy or physics. One simply cannot master the body of knowledge that quickly. 
\fs24 \

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 January, 1961 Willard Hotel Washington, D.C. 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b\fs96 \cf2 10\uc0\u8232 
\fs42\fsmilli21333 A Coming Out Party 
\f1\b0\fs24 \cf3 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Academic annual meetings differ from their counterparts in the other professions and the business world. Medical association meetings, for exam- ple, serve to announce advancements in equipment and therapies, provide a job fair, and create a forum for vendors to promote new techniques, software, and diagnostic equipment. Corporate meetings serve to instruct sales staff in new products and selling techniques, introduce new promotional material, and stimulate morale, money being the bottom line. Entertainment of some sort is provided, I believe, to stimulate the inevitably waning enthusiasm of the sales staff. Non-corporate business meetings, from my admittedly meager experience, are basically vacations. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Academic meetings have other functions, all associated with colleges and universities and research institutions. First, they provide undergraduates an opportunity to make their first presentations. Second, they provide junior faculty an opportunity to expand their resume, hopefully leading to tenure at their present or some future school. Vendors will be present, including book publishers and developers of whatever educational and research aids are appropriate to the given discipline. Job fairs are a major attraction, both for the recent graduates and hiring institutions. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The overall goal is to disseminate the latest research. Academia is specialized, with faculty and researchers in one area having limited knowledge of the latest results in other areas. A typical session will have, depending on the topic, 
\fs24 \
\'a9 Springer International Publishing AG 2017 
\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 71 
\f1\b0\fs24 L.M. Golden, 
\f2\i Never Split Tens!
\f1\i0 , DOI 10.1007/978-3-319-63486-9_10 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 \cf3 72 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 a couple dozen to maybe fifty attendees of those doing research in that area. Although it\'92s challenging enough to keep up in your own specialty, these meetings provide academics the opportunity to learn about advances in other specialties. From a social standpoint, meetings provide a common venue for former colleagues, either as students or faculty who have gone to other institu- tions, to reconnect. These meetings, as a result of these reconnections as well as the finely tuned senses of humor, sparkle with joviality. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The 1961 forty-fourth annual meeting of the American Mathematical Society in Washington, D.C., was typical of such gatherings, with one excep- tion. A young professor from MIT was presenting a paper on a strategy for the casino game of blackjack. Anticipation of this event had led to articles on January 25, the day before his Thursday, January 26, presentation, in the 
\f2\i Washington Post
\f1\i0 , 
\f2\i Boston Globe
\f1\i0 , and other papers, the content of which was quickly picked up via news services by thousands of other newspapers across the country. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 After his encounter with Baldwin\'92s system during his trip to South Lake Tahoe with Vivian, Allan, and Joan, Oakley had spent more than one hun- dred hours playing mock hands of blackjack, studying the effect of removing one or more of the Aces, then removing one or more of the twos, through all ten types of cards, the tens, Jacks, Queens, and Kings being all considered ten-value cards. It had taken Baldwin, Cantey, Maisel, and McDermott thousands of hours over one and one-half years, working evenings and week- ends, to develop just their basic strategy. At Oakley\'92s request, Baldwin had sent him the notebooks of his group\'92s calculations, enough to fill a card- board carton. As he studied them over weeks in the spring of 1959, Oakley became further convinced that the Four Horsemen hadn\'92t discovered the hidden secrets of the game. Their strategy, while sound, only considered the dealer\'92s up-card and the player\'92s two cards. He tried, working in a trancelike state, but he eventually admitted to himself that he had taken on a prob- lem too complex to solve in his lifetime, even in a simplified form. He gave up, a crushing decision for someone as competitive as Oakley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 After beginning his duties in the summer of 1959 in the C. L. E. Moore position at MIT, however, he was given permission to use their IBM high- speed 704 electronic digital computer at the MIT Computation Center. Now he had a real electronic computing machine with which to perform his calcu- lations. He was sure he\'92d have to make some approximations, but thought this venture would provide great scholarly rewards and great fun. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 First, he had to learn how to communicate with the 704 electronic digital computer. As many bright professors and graduate students in the sciences would do beginning in the 1960s, Oakley taught himself the FORTRAN 
\fs24 \

\fs32 programming language, short for \'93formula translation.\'94 The program was designed for mathematical analyses. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Using this great tool, much more sophisticated than the Friden electrical- mechanical calculating machines that Baldwin and his group had available, he joyfully and systematically programmed the effect of removing cards of various values from the deck. It became clear, without laboriously dealing mock hands for hundreds of hours, that removal of the low value cards, the twos through the sixes, provided an advantage to the player, and that removal of the ten- value cards and Aces provided an advantage to the house. Such a possibility had hit him after trying Baldwin\'92s system on that trip to South Lake Tahoe with Vivian, Allan, and Joan, reinforced, briefly, on his honeymoon night, and tantalizingly indicated in the more than the mentioned one hundred hours of dealing himself mock blackjack hands over eighteen months while he remained in L.A. after the Tahoe trip. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He had proceeded systematically. First, he wanted to use the 704 electronic computer to discover the improvements he could make over Baldwin and his group\'92s system. It was obvious that his use of a much more powerful comput- ing tool than their hand calculators would provide some if not many. He began with a full 52-card deck. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The necessary initial data were the probabilities of the dealer getting a given total for a given up-card, knowing that the dealer has to stand with a total of 17. If, for example, the dealer had an up-card of 5, Oakley had to determine the probability that after drawing the dealer would end up with a total of 17, 18, 19, 20, 21, or bust. To do this by hand to obtain results with reasonable preci- sion would require dealing thousands of hands. That\'92s the chore that Baldwin\'92s group faced. With FORTRAN and the 704, it required just twenty or so lines of code, and he could have the computer deal as many hands as he wanted until no significantly different result would be obtained by dealing more. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The results were startling. If the dealer\'92s up-card were a 4, 5, or 6, Oakley found that the dealer had a 40%, 43%, and 40% chance of busting, respec- tively, nearly half the time! If the up-card were a ten-value card or Ace, on the other hand, the chances were only 21% or 12% of busting, but the dealer in those cases had a 37% and 52% chance of obtaining a total of 21. With only this knowledge, the player would be encouraged to split and double down more frequently against a dealer showing an up-card of 4, 5, or 6. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 These results provided the foundation for all the subsequent calculations. To determine, for example, the player\'92s advantage for standing on a given total, one would simply add up the probabilities that the dealer would fall short of that total or bust and subtract the sum of the probabilities that the dealer would exceed that total. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 10 A Coming Out Party 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 73 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 74 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Thus, to determine the player\'92s advantage for standing with an 18 against the dealer\'92s up-card of 5, Oakley would add the probabilities that the dealer would obtain a 17 or bust, which he found to be 0.120 and 0.429, respectively, for a sum of 0.549, and subtract the probabilities that he would obtain a 19, 20, or 21, which he found to be 0.117, 0.105, and 0.106, respectively, for a sum of 0.328. The total would be 0.549 \uc0\u8722  0.328 = 0.221, indicating that the player had a 2.21% advantage by standing with an 18 against the dealer\'92s up-card of 5. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 In general the player also has the option of taking a card to improve his or her hand. Oakley therefore also programmed the 704 to determine the advan- tage the player would obtain by drawing cards. This would be a little more complicated. In our example, the player could draw an Ace, 2, or 3 to improve the hand, whereas drawing a card with a higher value would cause him or her to bust. The same summing of respective probabilities would yield the advantage. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 If, for example, the player were given a 3, giving him a total of 21, then the probability that he would win against the dealer\'92s up-card of 5 was the sum of the probabilities that the dealer would get a 17, 18, 19, 20, or bust times the probability that the player would actually get that 3, minus the probability that the player would bust, getting a card with a value greater than 3. The same calculation would have to be performed if he had drawn an Ace or a 2, and Oakley would then add the results of the three calculations to determine the advantage of drawing the card. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The difference between the advantages of drawing or standing would deter- mine the player\'92s strategy when faced, in this example, with an 18 against the dealer\'92s up-card of five. If the player didn\'92t bust, he had a further option of drawing another card or standing. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He similarly programmed the 704 to determine the advantages to the player of doubling down with given totals against the ten possible up-cards of the dealer, and the advantages to the player of splitting pairs, again against the ten possible up-cards of the dealer. Taken together, for every dealer up-card, the player with any two cards could now compare the advantages for splitting, doubling down, and drawing or standing to see which was the most advanta- geous strategy. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 No wonder it took Baldwin and his gang thousands of hours to do this by hand calculator. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Now Oakley faced a creative conundrum. The drawing and standing table was a matrix of ten possible dealer up-cards and nine possible player\'92s hands, 12 through 20, a player having less than 12 always taking another card. That was a total of ninety entries. The hard and soft doubling down tables and the splitting table added dozens more. Because he couldn\'92t expect anyone to 
\fs24 \

\fs32 memorize 150 to 200 decisions, he had to determine a means to organize the results into something more congenial to actual casino play. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He followed Baldwin\'92s lead. First, most of the decisions are obvious. You don\'92t double down on a sixteen. You don\'92t split fives. You don\'92t take a hit on a twenty. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He further simplified the results for actual casino use by combining all deci- sions for a given dealer up-card. The logic was simple. If the results showed that a player should hold with a 17 against the dealer\'92s up card of ten, then the results showed the obvious, that he should hold with an 18, 19, 20, or 21. If the results showed that the player should double down with a ten against the dealer\'92s up-card of nine, then he should also double down against the dealer\'92s up-card of two, three, four, five, six, seven, and eight. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 With these simplifications, the result could be summarized in concise tables that the player could memorize with what Oakley considered an acceptable, if not minimum, effort. Oakley, with the quick mind of the 704 as his partner, was revolutionizing the game of blackjack. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Taken together, Oakley\'92s more precise results showed that the player in a typical casino had a 0.12% advantage over the house. If he followed the direc- tions that Oakley summarized in the concise tables, for every $1000 wagered the player would in the long run make $1.20. Oakley referred to the refined system as the \'93basic strategy.\'94 The results showed that Baldwin\'92s group, while groundbreaking pioneers, had erred in their hand calculations slightly, having concluded that it was the house that had the small advantage. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 They had published a result that the house would have a 0.62% advantage, which they later found, as communicated by Wilbert Cantey to Oakley, was incorrect as a result of an arithmetic error and that the house advantage was actually only 0.32%. Oakley redid his computer calculation using the strategy they had published, and in fact found that their system provided an advantage to the player of 0.09%, remarkably close, attesting to the magnitude of their efforts, to Oakley\'92s electronic computer-generated results. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Because the system as published by Baldwin and his colleagues, and cor- rected, gave the house that small 0.32% advantage, a nearly even-money game much better for the player than any other casino game, the implication was clear that a skillful and somewhat lucky player could win at blackjack. Oakley had now shown that in the long run the player did not need skill or luck, but would indeed win, although at a miniscule rate. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 All that work to the good, Oakley\'92s results were basically a validation of Baldwin\'92s results, and Oakley had yet to incorporate blackjack not being, as he had noted to his friends after the marriage ceremony, a game of chance, that the trials in a game of sampling without replacement are not independent. He had to calculate using the 704 the effect of removing various cards. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 10 A Coming Out Party 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 75 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 76 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 He redid the analysis for removing one, two, three, and four of the fives. His results were astounding. The results showed that removing the fives provided up to a 3.6% advantage for the player. To any casual observer, using a favorite expression of scientists, this was a major, major advance. Wager $1000 and you expect to win in the long run $36. He referred to the strategy based on tracking the number of fives dealt from the deck as the \'93five-count strategy.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Astonished, invigorated, ebullient, he carried on relentlessly. He began to study the effect of removing ten-value cards from the 52-card deck. He would proceed just as he had with removing the fives, but now he would have to calculate the effect of removing 16 cards. As he had noted to Vivian in her nightgown, an abundance of ten-value cards and Aces compared to a neutral deck would increase the chances of blackjack. Because the dealer has to take a hit on 17, their presence increases his or her chances of busting. The player, who is not so constrained, can decline to take a hit holding cards which total, for example, 16, knowing that the deck is rich in ten-value cards. The pres- ence of those cards, Oakley thought, should also direct the player to double down and split more frequently. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 On the other hand, the presence of low-value cards would help the house. The dealer has to take a hit below seventeen. He or she would have a better chance of drawing one or more low-value cards, getting a 20 or 21. Oakley reasoned that when the deck is rich in low-value cards compared to a neutral deck, the player should double down and split less frequently, and take a card rather than stand with poor hands. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was becoming increasingly clear to Oakley that having the deck relatively rich in low-value cards decreases the player\'92s advantage, but having the deck relatively rich in high-value cards would increase his or her advantage. He had, accordingly, begun programming the 704 for a more advanced system based on tracking the ten-value cards that had been dealt. That would be called the \'93ten-count strategy.\'94 He estimated that, assuming it takes 10 seconds to play a hand in the casino, the 704 calculations, which played more than 30 billion hands, would have taken 10,000 man-years of actual casino play to accomplish. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was late 1960, however, and he wanted to share the results of his investi- gations based on removing the fives with colleagues. The completion of this analysis would have to wait. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He had submitted a paper on the \'93five-count strategy\'94 system to the American Mathematical Society annual meeting in Washington, D. C. The paper had been accepted for presentation. Oakley was only mildly surprised at this. After all, he was on staff at MIT, and the content of the paper was obviously not quackery, despite its dealing with a casino gambling game. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 An Associated Press editor in D. C. perusing the program for the meeting saw the beguiling if somewhat presumptuous title of Oakley\'92s presentation, \'93Fortune\'92s Formula: The Game of Blackjack,\'94 and directed AP staff in Boston to write an article about this young mathematician who promised gold that could be mined in every casino using only mental picks and shovels. A 
\f2\i Boston Globe 
\f1\i0 writer by the name of Dick Stewart called Oakley in the late evening of January 24 for a phone interview. A photographer was sent to his home. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 As I said, the next morning, Wednesday, January 25, a front page article appeared in the 
\f2\i Globe 
\f1\i0 and quickly thereafter newspapers across the country. The page 3 
\f2\i Washington Post 
\f1\i0 article by Thomas Wolfe the same day, based on a second telephone interview with Oakley, quoted Oakley as saying, \'93Frankly, I would welcome a wealthy backer or a gambling casino itself to finance me in what we might call the acid test of my theory. I haven\'92t been able to accumulate the capital to undertake it on my own.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Almost instantaneously, the phone calls from rabid gamblers began. They continued throughout the day and night from all over the country. By 4:00 a.m. Oakley was a wreck. He had found himself in a world as foreign as those of other planets of which he, as an astronomy buff, had wondered, but he knew he wanted to test the system and was going to present his paper and see the thing through. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The venue in the Willard Hotel where Oakley was to give his presentation was typical of academic meetings, a large ballroom divided into spaces for three separate academic presentations by portable partitions. It was in fact the appropriately named Grand Ballroom. On the slightly raised stage that served as a dais, a sign on the front of the lecture podium for the speaker provided the name of the meeting and the hotel, 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f2\i\fs29\fsmilli14667 \cf3 Willard Hotel\uc0\u8232 American Mathematical Society, 1961 Washington, D. C. 
\f1\i0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 A large screen behind the speaker displayed slides, and the speaker held a push button device to allow him to guide the slide projector to display the next or previous slides. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 What distinguished Oakley\'92s presentation from all others at this meeting was the number of those attending. More than one hundred people filled the chairs, others stood along the walls, and still others stood in the hall outside the room. This was, I repeat, a mathematics convention. The press coverage had generated major interest, and was to change the direction and import of Oakley\'92s life. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley stood behind the podium. The screen behind him displayed a slide showing tables of numbers and a large multi-colored pie chart. Each table had the title \'93The Five-Count Strategy for Blackjack.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The moderator for the session stood to his side. Incredibly rare for an academic meeting, two photographers were taking photographs of Oakley, their flashbulbs popping. Julian Braun sat in the first row, wearing a name badge identifying him as a registered meeting participant. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The question was, although the five-count strategy works, doesn\'92t the low rate of winning make it impractical,\'94 said Oakley, directing his comments to the audience. \'93It\'92s certainly true that the rate of winning is low. You have a three-and-one-half percent advantage over the house if all four five-value cards have been played.\'94 He pointed to the slide on the screen. \'93But as my table four shows, that only happens about six times out of one hundred hands that you play.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The session moderator pointed to a man in the audience.\uc0\u8232 \'93Question for Dr. Thorp.\'94\u8232 I always wondered at the use of the term \'93moderator\'94 to refer to the facili- 
\fs24 \

\fs32 tators of sessions at an academic meeting. It reminded me of the moderator rods inserted into nuclear reactors to absorb neutrons and control, that is, moderate, the fission rate and therefore power output, preventing annihila- tion of life on Earth. At meetings, the \'93moderator\'94 would presumably prevent uncontrolled disagreement between academics leading to intellectual rage and consequent cerebral meltdown. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The man in the audience spoke. \'93It\'92s easy to keep track of the number of fives that have been played,\'94 he said. \'93Wouldn\'92t appreciably more complicated systems be impossible to memorize? Would you have to essentially carry around a portable computer?\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Members of the audience laughed at the absurdity of such a concept. In the early 1960s the only computing machines were main frame computers which, because of the primitive electronics, literally filled entire rooms. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s a good point,\'94 admitted Oakley. \'93The question was essentially that if systems more sophisticated than the five count strategy get developed will the player be able to manage the calculations in his head or will a portable computing machine be necessary?\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The person asking the question nodded in agreement. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I think both will occur,\'94 continued Oakley. \'93I think the most complex systems that will be developed will require a portable computer. However, simplified versions of the systems would be amenable to mental calculation. In fact I\'92m now working on a system which I call the ten-count strategy. Here the player tracks all the ten-value cards that have been played and 
\fs24 \

\fs32 computes the ratio of non-ten-value cards to ten-value cards remaining in the deck. It\'92s more complicated than the five-count strategy, but it can still be memorized.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The moderator pointed to another man in the audience.\uc0\u8232 \'93Question for Dr. Thorp?\'94 he asked.\u8232 \'93Sure,\'94 said the man in the audience. \'93If you\'92re a rocket scientist.\'94\u8232 The audience laughed at this remark, not because it was inappropriate but 
\fs24 \

\fs32 because the man, in his non-academic manner of speaking, was thought by most in attendance to probably be correct. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Although those presenting papers or sponsoring booths typically must pay a fee to attend academic meetings, the publicity generated for Oakley\'92s presentation had persuaded the session organizers to allow the general public to attend. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93You admit there\'92s a low rate of winning, and maybe you\'92d need to carry around a machine on your back,\'94 the man continued. \'93Tell me, these electronic machine calculations are nice, but I don\'92t think it\'92ll work in practice. Have you left your ivory tower in the sky to really try any of your so-called systems in a real casino, Mr. Thorp? I mean, a real casino.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Members of the audience laughed and Oakley cringed. He had fought to keep his session open only to the press and academics, but the session organiz- ers prevailed. Oakley was now paying, having to endure the unenlightened commentary from one who was obviously not in the field of mathematics. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93No,\'94 said Oakley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The audience laughed again, this time with a bit of mockery. Oakley was getting upset. He felt that the moderator should have put this fellow\'92s questioning at rest. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Look, it\'92s mathematics,\'94 Oakley said, now feeling he was back in the neighborhood facing an imminent street fight. \'93The mathematical details will be in my paper. It works. I don\'92t have to prove anything to you. It works.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The audience responded to this with increasing laughter. A few catcalls were heard. The moderator decided it was time to terminate the presentation. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93One more question for Dr. Thorp,\'94 he said. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Julian had been listening to the interchange and had raised his hand. The moderator, noting that Julian wore a registration badge and was most likely not a layperson, pointed to him. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Can you estimate the errors in your expectation values resulting from the approximations that you made in your computer program?\'94 he asked. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley was glad to finally get an intelligent, relevant question. Hopefully the non-mathematician badgering was over. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 \'93Yeah,\'94 he said. \'93Actually, no. I would hope that the errors are small if they exist at all. My programs are free from bugs, I know that. And the system works. I can\'92t estimate the errors because more powerful systems haven\'92t been developed yet.\'94 Oakley was still irritated, and he was unhappy that he wasn\'92t able to properly answer the question. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Why don\'92t you do it?\'94 he challenged Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The audience felt that the prey had been cornered and was fighting for his life. They laughed and uttered more catcalls. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93With that,\'94 the moderator said, \'93We\'92re going to take a twenty-minute break before our next session.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The audience rose from their seats, and various groups began to converse. Oakley removed his slides from the slide projector, gathered his notes, and walked off the dais. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 A group of about fifteen to twenty descended on Oakley. Oakley looked at them hoping they weren\'92t vultures and hyenas coming to remove what was left on the bones. He had hoped the attacks were over. He feared, however, that they would begin anew. Some of them, judging from their ready notepads and pencils, were reporters. Julian stood to the side. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Dr. Thorp,\'94 said one of the reporters. At least he\'92s addressing me with respect, thought Oakley. \'93Joe Sherman, 
\f2\i The New York Times
\f1\i0 . You said that blackjack players could win regularly with your system. Can you translate that into cash?\'94 It was a reasonable question. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It depends on your minimum wager,\'94 replied Oakley. \'93If it\'92s one dollar, I think winning one hundred dollars in an hour playing heads-up against the dealer would be possible.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Dick Stewart, 
\f2\i Boston Globe
\f1\i0 ,\'94 said another. Oakley remembered the name as the reporter who had written a long piece about him and his work in the 
\f2\i Globe 
\f1\i0 that morning. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s a very optimistic claim,\'94 said Stewart. \'93I\'92m going to ask the same question that was asked before. You haven\'92t actually tried your system in a casino. Do you ever plan to?\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, hello,\'94 said Oakley, trying to stay on good terms, despite the potential dangerous implication of the question. \'93My biographer. Thanks for the article in today\'92s paper. At least that was nice. To answer your question, Dick, I haven\'92t tried the five-count strategy yet. I\'92ve tried the system developed by Baldwin and his colleagues and the results were very suggestive.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But you haven\'92t tried your system,\'94 persisted Stewart. \'93And what do you mean by \'91suggestive?\'92\'94 Oakley, to his dismay, had apparently found a new enemy. He tried humor. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I tried it in Lake Tahoe when I was a graduate student and didn\'92t lose all of my money,\'94 joked Oakley, smiling. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 At least the group laughed, and Stewart was silenced. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Actually, I made $8.50,\'94 Oakley continued. \'93But everyone else at the table lost all of theirs.\'94

\fs32 \'93Allen Weinrub. 
\f2\i Washington Post
\f1\i0 ,\'94 said another reporter. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Dr. Thorp, you haven\'92t tried your first system in a casino. Do you plan on soon trying out your new system in a casino? The ten-count strategy.\'94 That was a reasonable question. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I don\'92t think so. I\'92m a mathematician, not a gambler,\'94 Oakley replied. He had discovered an effective way of deflecting the embarrassing questions about trying out the system in the casinos. \'93It\'92s not my world. And remember, in the short run you can lose money. \fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Our editorial today read,\'94 continued Mr. Weinrub. He started to read, and it was apparent he was going in for the kill.

\fs32 \'93There\'92s a mathematician in town who says he has a system for beating blackjack. It reminds us of an ad we answered. Send only one dollar for a surefire weed killer. \fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley tried the humor-deflection technique.\uc0\u8232 \'93I\'92m mathematician, not a botanist.\'94\u8232 The group laughed at this witticism. His theory may not work, they might 
\fs24 \

\fs32 have thought, but he\'92s a good enough guy.\uc0\u8232 The first reporter, Sherman, wouldn\'92t relent.\u8232 \'93How do your colleagues at MIT view these pursuits of yours?\'94 he asked.

\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Well, they\'92re not willing to bankroll me, if that\'92s what you mean.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Some of the group laughed, but they were getting tired of Oakley acting like a sleaze politician, avoiding the questions. Some muttered non-niceties under their breath. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The students love it,\'94 said Oakley sensing the growing antagonism. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Stewart dove in. \'93Is it true, Professor Thorp,\'94 he said, with notable sarcasm in referring to Oakley by his professional title, \'93that your colleagues in the math department view your non-traditional research unfavorably and that far from getting tenure your contract for next year may not be . . .\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley had endured enough. The humor didn\'92t work. The sympathy- engendering comment about low faculty salaries didn\'92t work. The reference to his admiring students didn\'92t work. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93No more questions,\'94 he said, walking away. \'93Thank you very much.\'94 \'93Dr. Thorp . . .,\'94 said Sherman, wanting more.\uc0\u8232 \'93No more questions,\'94 said Oakley.

\fs32 \cf3 The three reporters thanked Oakley and let him go as the group dispersed. Oakley walked quickly away through the ballroom/lecture hall. Julian ran up to him and confronted him in the aisle. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I think you\'92re wrong,\'94 said Julian. \'93The approximations you had to make, even if incorrect by only two or five percent, would dominate your expectation values.\'94

\fs32 \cf3 The office of the MIT math department on the first floor of Building 2 was in room 2-106. This in itself would pose an enigma to the first-time visitor. Clearly, a room with the name 2-106 would be on the second floor. When Oakley first came to the campus, he expected the mathematics building to have the name of a wealthy New Englander who had endowed the university with a large gift. Instead, he found the name, literally Building 2, to be even less enchanting, if possible, than the name Mathematics Sciences building that he had known as a student and then in his teaching position at UCLA. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The office, 2-106, which was on the first floor, was decorated in neo- classical musky, with brown painted plaster walls with some narrow wood trim and several metal desks. A clock on the wall read 9:15 a.m. The calendar said it was Monday, as if those coming to the math department after the cold Massachusetts January weekend didn\'92t know. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 As all academic offices are, this office was functional. A swinging door would open at the far right end of a counter stretching from left to right a few feet beyond the door to the hallway. At the left end of the counter a large rect- angular metal box attached to the wall housed a mailbox. It had twenty-four mail slots with pieces of paper with the names of faculty members glued to the base of the respective slots. Underneath those slots were about forty smaller slots for the graduate students and post-doctoral fellows. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Magazines, letters, and note slips sat in the various mail slots. Oakley\'92s mail slot was empty. Oakley stood at the mailbox. He stuck his hand into his mail slot and moved it around to make sure no piece of mail was lying flat in it. Jane Rose, the department secretary, sat at a desk on the other side of the

\fs32 The blackboard in Lecture Hall 112, Building 2 \'96 officially room 2-112 \'96 was full of drawings sketched with chalk of square grids similar to rectangular graph paper. One drawing had a conically shaped figure drawn to simulate it penetrating through the graph paper, with the words \'93wormhole\'94 identifying the cone. Next to it was a drawing with the graph paper drawn as if it were bending around on itself, with the wormhole cone shrinking and then enlarg- ing and opening back onto a different part of the drawing. \fs24 \

\fs32 A clock hung on the wall above the center of the blackboard. It was five minutes to eleven o\'92clock. Julian, cradling a bulky, large manila envelope in his lap, sat in the back row.
