\fs32 \'93The two spaces are equivalent mathematically, simple topological transfor- mations,\'94 said Oakley. \'93Now, physically, they represent different phenomena, as you can see.\'94 He pointed to the first diagram. \'93Here the singularity is iso- lated. \fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Forty-four,\'94 he announced to the cheering crowd. \'93My all-time record is one hundred and forty-eight. Two triple nineteens and a double seventeen. \fs32 His office typified that of the creative academic intellect. Shelves were full of journals, books, reprints of articles, and manuscripts, and his desk was piled high with journals. \fs32 \'93First,\'94 said Julian, \'93the 7044 has much larger working memory than the 704 you used. \fs32 \cf3 \'93It also permits more nested DO LOOPS so I can use those variables of more dimensions that you could with the 704,\'94 said Julian, as if speaking of a child of whom he was proud, but a bit apprehensive about Oakley\'92s response to his work. \'93I can store simultaneously the number of cards played, the num- ber of hands played, the number of cards of each value played, the number of unseen cards that have been dealt, and so on. \fs32 twos through sixes the \'91lows\'92 and the ten-value cards and Aces the \'91highs.\'92 In other words, although removing the fives from the deck provided the greatest advantage to the player, now we group the twos, threes, fours, and sixes in the same class. Dubner kept track of the number of each type of card that was played and seen and then subtracted the number of lows that were left to be played from the number of highs left to be played. It\'92s the difference between the two types of cards that remain that he found to be significant. It\'92s the basis of what Harvey Dubner called the \'91Basic Hi-Lo Strategy.\'92\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Nice. In effect, he said that all the lows had the same effect and all the highs had the same effect,\'94 observed Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s exactly right,\'94 responded Oakley. \'93Now, can we quantify this rather than subtracting the number of each class of card dealt? I said sure, it\'92s easier to just assign values to the cards. I assign a point value of +1 to the twos through sixes as they are played and seen by the player and one of \uc0\u8722 1 to the ten-value cards and Aces as they are played and seen. What I call the point total or total point count, or just point count for short, is the arithmetic sum of the point values of the cards that were played and seen, the total point value of the cards. In the end, you remember only one number, the sum.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Instead of two numbers, the number of highs and lows remaining,\'94 noted Julian. \'93Easier to do.\'94

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley had thought of this himself. \'93Sure,\'94 he agreed. \'93The five-count strategy was based on their absence providing the best advantage to the player of any card, so I could assign, say, +2 to the fives, and because the absence of Aces is disadvantageous I could assign \uc0\u8722 2 to them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But, will this really generate an improved discrimination in the quality of the deck? I don\'92t think so. First, they burn a card, which you can\'92t see to include in the count. That by itself introduces an uncertainty. If it\'92s a five or an Ace, the error could be plus or minus two instead of plus or minus one. The mistakes you\'92ll inevitably be making in counting the cards introduces more uncertainty in the count if the absolute value of some are increased, and mak- ing the system more complex will tire the player and increase the frequency of those errors.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But if the errors are random and relatively few, it shouldn\'92t affect the play- er\'92s expectation values.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah. In the long run, but not necessarily in the short run, right? In short, it\'92s what I think is just an unjustifiably more difficult calculation,\'94 he concluded. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Which you want to do in your head,\'94 noted Julian.

Julian knew how a think tank works, and IBM was to a large degree a think tank. The algorithms that he was developing and using to analyze blackjack might indeed be applicable to work that IBM might now or in the future con- sider relevant. IBM was a business, however, not a university where aca- demics can pursue their research interests, no matter how unconventional, arcane, or even unpopular with colleagues they may be. At least, Julian had repeatedly reassured himself, his supervisors had never complained about his hobby.

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley sat in his swivel chair. Then he suddenly pumped his fist in the air in exultation. \'93Holy, holy, holy, Moses . . . Cheese . . . Cow . . . holy Toledo. Ohhh., Julian!\'94 For an atheist, Oakley had a lot of gods.

\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The beauty of a 7044,\'94 said Julian, modestly. He was being more than modest, of course. He wanted to ensure that Oakley knew that he was an indispensable partner. \fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley sat in his swivel chair and leaned back. He stared at the wall. They had created a monster, if a mathematical rather than biological one. He got back up and walked to the blackboard. He sketched out a rectangular coor- dinate system and wrote \'93
\f2\i E
\f1\i0 \'94 next to the ordinate scale and \'93
\f2\i I
\f1\i0 \'94 next to the abscissa scale. He put a minus sign below the abscissa scale to the left of the origin and a positive sign below the scale to the right of the origin. He drew a continuous line, beginning as a concave upward curve in the third quadrant, straightening out as it approached and passed through the origin, and then continuing as a straight line at close to a 45-degree angle upwards into the first quadrant. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93If I made a graph of the expectation value for a one-dollar bet as a func- tion of the high-low index, it would certainly be an increasing function, if you follow the strategy decisions,\'94 said Oakley pointing to the graph, in a capsule summary of their work. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Julian started to make a comment, but Oakley knew what was coming. He raised his hand. \'93I said \'91if you follow the strategy decisions.\'92 The index will be negative as frequently as it will be positive, but the player will be changing the strategy. You\'92ve got to follow the system.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Ah, yeah,\'94 said Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93And it provides a guide to betting,\'94 Oakley added, in a trance. You bet one-half of the high-low index.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That\'92s very interesting,\'94 said Julian. He pointed to the formula Oakley had written on the blackboard. \'93As the deck gets richer in cards favoring the player, he or she should increase the bet, and this provides the formula,\'94 Julian joyfully announced, becoming unable to control his enthusiasm at their mutual tutorial. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Fortune\'92s formula,\'94 added Oakley. \'93Better than the betting concept I had for the simple point count where, in its simplest form, you just bet the total point count, or your minimum bet if the count is zero or negative.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Julian smiled, remembering that phrase as part of the title of Oakley\'92s paper to the American Mathematical Society meeting. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Lower your bet to a minimum for low or negative values of the index, reducing your expected loss, and increase it when the index is positive, increas- ing your expected winnings,\'94 instructed Oakley. \'93This follows what Harvey suggested in his Hi-Lo system. He suggested $1 bets when the hi-lo index was small or negative, $5 bets when the index was between 0.05 and 0.10, $10 bets when the index was between 0.10 and 0.20, and $20 bets when the index was greater than 0.20.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93So he basically said bet half of the upper boundary of the interval of the Hi-Lo Index, multiplied by 100,\'94 concluded Julian. \'93Again, our results will be more detailed.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley pointed to the curved line he had drawn on the graph, and drew a second curved line. It began from the origin but curved above the first line he had drawn. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Changing your bets with changing the high-low index increases the expec- tation values even further. I developed a rough scheme on increasing the bet with the ten-count strategy system, but Baldwin\'92s system, my basic strategy, the five-count, and even the simple point count essentially said just to raise the bet when the deck became favorable. Basically raise it as high as possible, except it may tip off the house when you consistently win a large fraction of those large bets,\'94 he said, finishing the lecture to his one-man class on the subject of the high-low index. He sat down, slapping his hands together to dissipate the chalk dust. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Julian walked over to the dart board against the wall and pulled out Oakley\'92s darts. He walked back about ten feet and threw the darts one by one trying for the bull\'92s eye. The darts stuck on the three, eleven, and eight sectors. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93A twenty-two,\'94 said Oakley. \'93Not bad for a first try, Julian. With a little more practice you could be named rookie of the year.\'94 He pointed to his desk. \'93I want to show you something.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Now it was time for Oakley\'92s show and tell. He carefully pulled a writing tablet out from the pile of books on his desk and placed it beside Julian\'92s note- book. Julian stared in amazement, not only that Oakley could find the tablet but that the pile of books did not come toppling down. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93These are the tables in the format I devised for my recalculation of Baldwin\'92s system using the 704, what I call the basic strategy, and for my ten- count strategy. The possible dealer\'92s up-cards from two through Ace are along the top row and the player\'92s possible two-card totals starting from twelve up to nineteen are along the left-hand column,\'94 said Oakley, pointing to the top page of the tablet. The entries in the matrix direct the player to draw or

\fs32 \cf3 stand. The cell being darkened means that\'92s the minimum standing total,\'94 said Oakley in lecture mode.

\fs32 \'93The Four Horsemen provided strategy charts in their book with sections on drawing and standing, doubling down, and splitting pairs,\'94 observed Julian. \'93Yours is similar.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah, I know,\'94 said Oakley. Here, see, this is the hard drawing and stand- ing table for the basic strategy. With the player holding a 13 or more against the dealer\'92s 3 you stand. If you have less than 13, you take a card. Same hold- ing 12 against her 3.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The dreaded 12 and 13,\'94 moaned Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93And 14 and 15 and 16,\'94 Oakley added, \'93the heinous, malevolent, mar- ginal hands,\'94 Oakley continued, apparently ascribing free will to the cards. \'93In the comparable tables I\'92ll create for the complete point count system, I\'92ll put the values of the high-low index to guide the standing decision in the cells where the decision isn\'92t clear-cut and obvious, that is, where the value would not be either a very large positive or negative number. If the player\'92s high-low index exceeds the value in the cell, he holds. If it\'92s lower than the value in the cell, he takes a card.\'94

\fs32 \'93This is very clever, Ed.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Harvey Dubner presented his results more or less verbally,\'94 continued Oakley. \'93I think presenting them in this tabular form will be easier for the player to use.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He turned over the top sheet to reveal a second. He let Julian stare at it for a moment. \'93This is a similarly laid out table for hard doubling, and here\'92s the table for soft doubling. That\'92s the two-card total for hard doubling, combination of Ace and another card for soft doubling, and the various pairs for the pair splitting table. \fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I think you\'92ll find that most of the strategy decisions will be the same as in the less sophisticated systems, Baldwin\'92s, your basic strategy, and your five- count strategy,\'94 said Julian with the experience of one who had performed the most extensive programming of the game. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It\'92s the marginal, difficult decisions that will be decided, based on your results, by the complete point count system,\'94 said Oakley, almost bragging, although a large portion of the credit was due, as he knew, to Julian. \'93Yeah,\'94 said Julian pointing to an entry in Oakley\'92s table for holding and drawing. \'93Here, for example, if you\'92ve got a total of 12 against the dealer\'92s 6, or a 13 against her 2.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Or double down with a 9 against her 3, or an 11 against her Ace,\'94 said Oakley referring to his second page. \'93The basic strategy says double down holding an 11 and, depending on the dealer\'92s up-card, usually a 10 and some- times a 9. The index will uncover other favorable situations.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93This is a much more powerful system.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah. These are difficult decisions for the player.\'94 Oakley showed Julian the fourth page again. \'93Here, the splitting table for the basic strategy says to split fours against a five. I\'92m sure the complete point count will provide guides to split against her 4 and 6, too. It\'92ll provide guides for standing or drawing, doubling down, and splitting that a player using the basic strategy would never consider.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Even splitting tens, perhaps,\'94 suggested Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The basic strategy says never split tens,\'94 noted Oakley. \'93But with a high value of the index, indicating many tens and Aces remain in the deck, that might be a good move. The ten-count strategy uncovered such cases.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But you never tried it out in the casinos,\'94 remarked Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Nope. I never tried it out in the casinos,\'94 said Oakley. \'93But that\'92s going to change.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93From your mouth to God\'92s ears,\'94 said Julian, repeating one of his mother\'92s favorite phrases. \'93But you\'92re right, winning these marginal cases should mark- edly increase the player\'92s advantage,\'94 Julian summarized. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The super-sensitive sense of the humor of the brilliant mind was manifested as Oakley laughed at what 99.999% of the population would consider an extremely dry, humorless statement. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That is the real advantage of the complete point count system,\'94 chuckled Oakley. \'93With the 704, I found an overall player\'92s advantage of 0.12% play- ing my recalculated basic strategy, and up to a 3.6% advantage playing the five-count, when all the fives have been played. I recalculated Baldwin\'92s strat- egy and found a 0.09% advantage to the player. Using your results, I wouldn\'92t be surprised to find advantages of 5 to 10% or more using the complete point count system.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Or 15% or 20%,\'94 laughed Julian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Hey, you want to get some tea or coffee?\'94 asked Oakley. They both needed a break.

\fs32 \cf3 Boston University, Boston College, Harvard, MIT, Northeastern, Tufts, the Berklee School of Music, renowned for its jazz program, Radcliffe, Smith, Brandeis, Amherst, Simmons, Wellesley, U Mass, and other colleges and uni- versities make Boston, Cambridge, and the Boston area their home. Even with all the Irish alcoholics lying in the streets, it was always a great place to go to college. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The tens of thousands of professors, staff, and students, though, have a demand for housing, and, although many students live in the dorms or frater- nities during their freshman and perhaps sophomore years, by their upperclass years many look for apartments or houses to rent. For a newly arrived faculty member, especially if married, finding congenial, affordable, and relatively nearby housing can be challenging. College and university housing offices can help find nearby apartments, but for Vivian and Oakley, the options were not acceptable. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 They were able, however, to find a nice, small rental house in Brookline, a western suburb of Boston just across the Charles River and a bit west from Cambridge and MIT. It was a quick ride on the Metropolitan Transit Authority, the MTA of the George O\'92Brien themed celebrated song by the Kingston Trio, to the Massachusetts Avenue stop and then either a bus ride or a hitchhike across the river. If Oakley had been a bike rider, he could have made the trip on Beacon Street, past Fenway Park, and across the Harvard Bridge on Massachusetts Avenue directly into campus in 15 minutes. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Hitchhiking across the Harvard Bridge was not fraught with the usual hitchhiking anxiety of not succeeding and, in this case, missing classes. The scads of students standing there, thirty at a time, thumbs in the air, posed no

\fs32 Their oval dining room table was covered with white linen this special eve- ning. \fs32 The little party to both entertain and interrogate Manny Kimmel was jovial enough. Oakley, as the host, sat in the chair at one end of the table. Julian and Nancy sat to his left. Pretend you\'92re ski bunnies or something, you know, there for skiing. Wear a parka. You know, fit in. Or booze hounds. For the free drinks. Slobber it on you, so you reek of the stuff.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Vivian and I are musicians,\'94 Oakley said. He wanted to make sure to include his wife, and spoke the words loudly enough so she, still scrounging for some chocolate in the kitchen, could hear it. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah, that\'92s good,\'94 responded Manny. \'93You came to hear the music or something.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Well, how do you protect yourself against the false shuffle?\'94 persevered Julian. He was aware of the impending nuptial crisis, but he hadn\'92t driven in from Chicago to watch a parade of beer and chocolates. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah, sorry, a problem. But it\'92s hard to keep the meld if you shuffle more than twice. And you gotta be sure, see, that they cut the deck first, shuffle each part, and then put the deck together and shuffle again. That pretty much wipes out the melds unless the guy\'92s really good.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley looked toward the kitchen. Either Vivian was sitting somewhere taking a break or was digging very deeply in drawers looking for those Snickers or M and M\'92s. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 \'93Bottom dealing?\'94 he asked. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93No,\'94 answered Manny. \'93See, they burn a card but flip it first. So, see, the bottom card is turned over. Too obvious, anyway. Guy turns his palm, looks at the bottom card. Nah. That don\'92t happen no more. You\'92re not going to have to worry about that when you\'92re playing. You and the wife are musicians. That\'92s a good cover.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93What do you mean?\'94 asked Oakley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93You don\'92t have to worry about that,\'94 repeated Manny. \'93They ain\'92t inter- ested in cheating kid musicians. Pretend you\'92re there to get a gig.\'94

\fs32 \'93I thought you wanted me to teach you the system. Look, I\'92ve got to prove to all these doubters that the system works, but you\'92ve got to play.\'94 Oakley was starting to get an empty feeling in his stomach. \'93And I\'92m uncomfortable with such sums,\'94 he added. \'93Ten thousand is enough.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93No no no no no no no,\'94 said Manny in a near stammer. \'93They know who I am. And I can\'92t do your math stuff. My nephew Seymour, he\'92s a math genius, too. He could do it, but not me.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He stared at Oakley. \'93You\'92re going to do this. Me and some of my best friends, we provide the cash and take 90% of the profit. It\'92s a business deal. Ten thousand? That\'92s okay. You\'92re doubling it. We make the profit, and you try out your system.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Manny Kimmel, born Emmanuel Kimmel, was a bookie and gangster in New Jersey and New York dating from the Depression. Unknown to Oakley, he had been considered a \'93notable underworld figure\'94 for decades. He was considered to have run the largest handle horseracing book in a crime- ridden, book-happy, corrupt New York City. He worked with the single big- gest racketeer and bootlegger in Newark, Abner \'93Longy\'94 Zwillman, a feared Prohibition gangster, leasing his garages to Zwillman for storage of liquor dur- ing Prohibition. So well-known were his transgressions that the FBI tracked him for his relationships with the mob and eventually persuaded him to tes- tify against Zwillman, his former associate. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He and his wife Pauline (Polk) had founded the Kinney Parking Company, a prominent national chain of parking lots and garages. The name was sug- gested by its first location having been on Kinney Street in Newark. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 That Manny had adopted this name to hide his Jewish identity was the 
\f2\i modus operandi 
\f1\i0 of American Jews from the turn of the twentieth century to its end, a \'93time-honored tradition\'94 of American Jews anglicizing both their given and business names to hide their Jewish identity. This was done not only to 
\fs24 \

\fs32 avoid anti-Semitic persecution and discrimination but also to fulfill a desire to be accepted and assimilated into American society. Although they still read newspapers published in the Yiddish of Eastern Europe, supporting dozens across the country, the pride of first generation-born American Jews was to learn and become fluent in English. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Yet, they still faced persecution and discrimination. Most professions were to some degree closed to Jews, including architecture, medicine, law, and vet- erinary practice. The famous Cornell University veterinary school, for exam- ple, through the early 1940s, allowed only one Jew in an entering class. Jewish animal lovers reacted by founding a veterinary school at what was to become Middlesex University in Waltham, Massachusetts, with a faculty of Jews who had fled Germany. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Colleges and universities, including most of the elite universities, even until the 1980s, had such Jewish \'93quotas,\'94 severely restricting the number of Jewish students they would admit, previous academic success and future academic promise being irrelevant. With programs in medicine, pharmacy, podiatry, and veterinary medicine, as well as the liberal arts, Middlesex was the only university in the United States at which the medical and vet- erinary schools did not impose a quota on Jews. The American Medical Association refused to grant accreditation to its med school, which its non- Jewish founder partially attributed to institutional anti-Semitism. After the founder\'92s death, Middlesex evolved into the predominantly Jewish Brandeis University in 1947. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Socially, Jews were isolated. The Jews who entered college would form their own fraternities and sororities, the Sigma Chi\'92s and Tri Delt\'92s not rushing Jews for members. Out of college, with admission to social and athletic Clubs denied, the Jews formed their own. Julian Braun\'92s parents belonged to the Covenant Club, formed by Eastern European Jews. Chicago Jews of German descent formed and joined the Standard Club. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 One of the few fields open to Jews was physics, that not being a calling of most non-Jews. With the field open, most of the breathtaking develop- ments of the 20th century were made by Jews. The names of Jews, Hans Bethe, Albert Einstein, Wolfgang Pauli, Richard Feynman, Albert Michelson, Max Born, James Franck, Emilio Segre, Arno Penzias, Murray Gell-Mann, Leon Lederman, Niels Bohr, Steven Weinberg, and others, are inscribed in the listings of Nobel Prize winners in gross disproportionality to their numbers. J. Robert Oppenheimer, the genius who directed in the Manhattan Project, which contained the greatest assemblage of physics intellects ever assem- bled, was Jewish. This success aside, Jews were denied entry into most other American professions. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 12 Forbidden Fruit 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 109 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 110 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Jews, indeed, created their own American industries where they would not suffer discrimination in opportunity and hiring. Three of the five American- born entertainment industries were of Jewish origin. New York City Jewish veterans of vaudeville performed in the resorts of the Catskill Mountains, and there, in the Borscht Belt, stand-up comedy was born. Those with a musi- cal and theatrical bent founded musical theatre, an outgrowth of Eastern European Yiddish Theatre and the source of the popular music canon known as the \'93great American songbook.\'94 The motion picture industry was founded by Jews who moved to southern California. Of the five American-born enter- tainment industries, only vaudeville and jazz, created in New Orleans by blacks, were not of Jewish origin. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 That discrimination in the professions and colleges continued after World War II. The 500,000 American Jews, an absolutely remarkable half of all Jewish males between the ages of 18 and 50, who served, fought, and became injured or died in the war returned to a bigoted America. Many, facing this discouraging fact, opted to devote themselves to the creation of a Jewish homeland. Israel was built on the intellect and industry of transplanted Jews. In a significant way, American bigotry led to its creation, existence, and flour- ishing as a prominent world center in agriculture, military technology, and salt water reclamation, and as well as classical music and the graphic arts. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 To avoid discrimination as well as to seek assimilation, American Jews changed their last names. Cohen became Kahn and Cahn. Greenbaum became Greene. Goldstein became Golden. Shapiro became Shepard. Entertainers could be partially excused for the sake of the marquee, Cyd Charisse adopt- ing that name from her given Tula Finklea, Tony Curtis having been born Bernard Schwartz, Kirk Douglas having been born Issur Danielovitch, and Jack Benny having been born Benny Kubelsky, but it didn\'92t hurt bookings if they had what they referred to as \'93goyish,\'94 that is, gentile, sounding names. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Learning the lesson, Jewish parents would forego the traditional old- country Jewish first names. They would try to avoid the Irving\'92s, Abe\'92s, Sam\'92s, Ben\'92s, Nathan\'92s, Max\'92s, Bernard\'92s, Myron\'92s, Meyer\'92s, Hymen\'92s, Saul\'92s, Bertha\'92s, Tillie\'92s, Libby\'92s, and Rose\'92s of their fathers and mothers and name their children Elyse, Iris, Renee, Brad, and Todd. Because Jews name their children after the Jewish name of deceased loved ones, this necessitated some imaginative phonemic manipulation. Parents would choose an English name of \'93Larry\'94 or \'93Leslie\'94 to honor and memorialize \'93Eliezer.\'94 Similarly, \'93Moshe\'94 would be remembered as \'93Morris\'94 or \'93Mark,\'94 \'93Chanah\'94 as \'93Anne,\'94 \'93Miriam\'94 as \'93Marla,\'94 \'93Binyomin\'94 as \'93Bruce,\'94 and \'93Pinchus\'94 as \'93Paul.\'94 Emmanuel Kimmel preferred to be called Manny. As I said, he named his business \'93Kinney,\'94 an Irish surname. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 In the case of our family, both Vivian\'92s father, Abe Sinetar, a businessman, and her mother, Adele Steiner, were Jewish. Because Jews ascribe the religion of the mother to the children, that connection being documentable, all of us Thorp kids are Jewish, and we were raised Jewish. My maternal grandpar- ents\'92 names \'93sound\'94 Jewish, but \'93Vivian\'94 was a good old American-sounding name. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Whether or not his associates knew his ethnic background, Manny\'92s suc- cessful resume included being a bookie in his younger days and running the numbers game and other books in New Jersey. He was knowledgeable, con- nected, and passionate about his \'93career.\'94 This passion even led him to own several racehorses. By the time he met Oakley he had never served a day of time, and he never would. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley paused, thunderstruck, to borrow Robinson Crusoe\'92s description at seeing Friday\'92s footprints in the sand, at this unexpected diatribe from what he was hoping would be his bankrolling bookie buddy. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It\'92s me trying it or nothing, huh?\'94 he asked Manny. He turned to Julian. \'93Spring break, Julian?\'94\uc0\u8232 Julian nodded his head affirmatively, then checked in with Nancy. \'93Nancy?\'94 Vivian chose this moment to return with a candy bar. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93The Three Musketeers,\'94 Nancy said, referring either to the candy bar or to the trio of Oakley, Julian, and Manny. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I can\'92t gamble,\'94 Oakley reconsidered.\uc0\u8232 \'93You never played blackjack?\'94 said an incredulous Manny.\u8232 \'93In grad school, we went up to Tahoe once, but not with these kind of 
\fs24 \

\fs32 stakes.\'94\uc0\u8232 \'93What\'92s the diff?\'94 said Manny. \'93It\'92s the same game. You want to try out 
\fs24 \

\fs32 your system, then we give you the cash and you do the playing.\'94 He stared at Oakley, who, by his pursed eyebrows, showed he wasn\'92t convinced. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Hey, Thorp,\'94 yelled Manny. He slammed his fist on the table, causing the coffee and tea cups and their saucers to jiggle. \'93You want to prove it works, then you play,\'94 his voice was suddenly raspy, showing that years of screaming had taken its toll on his vocal cords. \'93I didn\'92t come up here to take no crap.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Oh, Edward.\'94 She had never heard this chapter of the Thorp family his- tory before.

\fs32 \cf3 Vivian must have said \'93okay.\'94\uc0\u8232 Manny had secured lodging for all of them, Vivian and Oakley, Allan and 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Joan, Julian and Nancy, as well as an enormous luxury suite for himself and his nieces, at the most prestigious gambling location in Reno, the Mapes Hotel. The first high-rise hotel/casino complex in the entire state of Nevada, located on the northeast corner of Virginia Street and the Truckee River, it was the destina- tion of both celebrity visitors and performing acts booked in the top floor The Sky Room. Marilyn Monroe, Clark Gable, Frank Sinatra, Milton Berle, Jimmy Durante, Sammy Davis, Jr., and many others had either stayed or performed there, hanging out during leisure hours at the ground floor Lamplighter Bar. Vivian and Oakley\'92s act would never have been booked at the Mapes Hotel. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was just as well. To keep business and pleasure separate, Manny and Oakley agreed that they should not stay where they would gamble. They would gamble at Harold\'92s Club. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The sign above the stage proclaimed the name of its more modest entertain- ment venue to be the \'93Pair-o\'92-Dice Lounge.\'94 Below that was a smaller sign reminding the potentially inebriated patron that the lounge was in Harold\'92s Club.

\fs32 If you challenged the speed limit, it was a short 31\uc0\u8260 4 hour drive from Berkeley to Reno through Donner Pass, that of the ill-fated Donner party pio- neers, and over the Sierra Nevada. This was a scenic California trip. Highway 80 passes through Sacramento to the Donner area and, having negotiated Donner Pass, a ride over the highest point along Highway 80 of the Sierras whose treacherously steep descent to the east requires tire chains and cau- tiously low speeds during the winter, you drive a few more miles along the north shore of Donner Lake into Truckee and then follow a serpentine path through the Truckee River mini-valley into Reno. You frequently drive next to the river itself, crossing it back and forth no less than eight exhilarating times before reaching the gambling mecca. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Actually, to tell the truth, the historical Donner Pass lies about a mile south of Highway 80, but everyone refers to the route as going through Donner Pass anyway. It makes it sound like you\'92re more of a brave old West pioneer and you gain the sympathy of those who have had to install chains. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan had learned well that after a winter storm, the residents set up shop at the top of the pass selling chains and chain-installation services. You have to decide whether to spend the $10 for installation alone or freeze your hands putting on chains yourself. The residents of the Donner area must either be proud of its name or aware that it may attract historically literate tourists who may or may not be considering cannibalism. It contains a Donner Avenue, Donner Lake Road, Donner Pass Road, Donner Creek, Donner Lake, and Donner Memorial State Park, in addition to numerous cafes and other busi- nesses with the name. The traveler obtains some designation relief reaching Truckee a few miles to the east. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 It\'92s a great tune, and Vivian and Oakley performed it professionally. The house trio looked at each other, smiling. This was a duo with whom they\'92d like to, as they say, go on the road.

\fs32 Manny and his nieces, Allan and Joan, Julian and Nancy, and Bernie applauded. \fs24 \

\fs32 leading to the Great Depression, the Kurtzmans lost the building, and the entire clan, one by one, had relocated to Los Angeles. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Pauline had two siblings. In a story representative of Jewish migration to the United States from Eastern Europe around the turn of the century, older sister Gussie had moved to Chicago. Pauline and her brother Oscar, being younger, had remained in Eastern Europe, perhaps Poland, with their parents, but

\fs32 \cf3 they immigrated to Chicago at a young age after their mother died and their father remarried, to a woman with a sizable number of children. Their step- mother was not particularly pleasant to her stepchildren Pauline and Oscar. In Chicago, they were reunited with Gussie. Oscar would also move with the Kurtzmans to Los Angeles. Their move was accompanied with mixed feelings, Pauline and Oscar leaving their sister Gussie, and adopted Chicago in spite of the opportunities and better weather of southern California. Many Jews were moving to the west coast for the better weather. The various Kurtzman brothers would go into business together and eventually thrive in Oriental importing and mens\'92 wear businesses. Oscar would work for two of them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Bernie lived in a modest home in Los Angeles off of Robertson Blvd. He was a successful garment salesman at an upscale Los Angeles men\'92s store, and his brother became a dentist and lived in Sherman Oaks. Both married Los Angeles-raised Jewish women. Uncle Phil, as he was known, remained close to the families of Bernie and Myron and worked for Universal Studios in the wardrobe department. He eventually married a woman he had been \'93dating\'94 when he was in his 70s. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 When he was in his 50s, Bernie\'92s two daughters and his son now grown, Bernie and his wife Freda decided to move to Nevada. It was going to be just a temporary change of life. Las Vegas was too frenetic for their gentle, homey personalities, 
\f2\i haymisha
\f1\i0 , in Yiddish, so they decided on the Reno area. Although he had planned on an early retirement, he got a little antsy and fell into the lounge manager position at Harold\'92s. He enjoyed the job, particularly helping young talent advance their careers. He felt that Oakley and Vivian, at this point, needed further preparation. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 The afternoon following their pseudo audition, Oakley was ready to set the foundation for trying out the complete point count system that he and Julian had developed. First, as they had done at Barney\'92s in South Lake Tahoe, they would put the casino off guard by playing like unsophisticated young kids, making stupid moves and betting low stakes. Here, though, they would focus on using irrational so-called systems. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The four, Allan, Joan, Vivian, and Oakley, sat at a blackjack table at Harold\'92s. The other seats were vacant. It was an understated place, with muted green and gray colors. Oakley would get to like Harold\'92s Club both because of the quiet atmosphere and because he would always seem to win there. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Each player had bet a single $1 chip, placed neatly in the very center of their betting squares. Cocktail glasses and glasses of water stood in front of each position. The cocktail glasses were full. Even the water was barely touched. These poor young neophytes were so concerned about losing their weekly lunch money, they weren\'92t even drinking a glass of water. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Two cards, a six of Diamonds and a five of Clubs, lay face up on the table in front of the first seat to the left of the dealer, Allan\'92s place. Two cards lay on the table in front of each of the other three occupied seats, all face down. Two cards, one face up and one face down, lay in front of the dealer. The up card was a seven of Hearts. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Jackie, a female dealer, about 25, stared at Allan. Clive, the pit boss, stood next to Jackie, also observing Allan. 
\fs24 \
\'a9 Springer International Publishing AG 2017 
\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 127 
\f1\b0\fs24 L.M. Golden, 
\f2\i Never Split Tens!
\f1\i0 , DOI 10.1007/978-3-319-63486-9_14 \

\fs24 Never Split Tens

\fs32 \cf3 Allan\'92s eyes were closed as he opened a Bible to a random page. He stuck his forefinger onto the open page of the Bible, then opened his eyes and looked at the Bible. He uttered a sigh and showed the configuration of the Bible pages and his finger to Jackie. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Hebrew words,\'94 he said. \'93I stand.\'94\uc0\u8232 "Religious, huh?\'94 concluded Jackie.\u8232 \'93I believe that the Lord watches over me and guides me,\'94 Allan said righ- 
\fs24 \

\fs32 teously. \'93If my finger points at an English word, I take a card. If it\'92s a Hebrew word, I stand.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93But you\'92ve got eleven,\'94 protested Jackie.\uc0\u8232 \'93The Bible says stand,\'94 said our sanctimonious one.\u8232 \'93Oh, God,\'94 said Jackie in understandable disbelief.\u8232 Allan glared at her. This was a firm believer in the ways of the Lord, and 
\fs24 \

\fs32 any heretical behavior would, if he had any means of communicating with the Supreme Being, be severely punished, if not in this life, then in the afterlife. Allan was proving to be a good actor. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Sorry,\'94 apologized Jackie, not wanting to irritate a client. She cleared her throat. \'93That\'92s quite a system.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It\'92s the best,\'94 Allan guaranteed her. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He communicated his conviction with a mixture of near sexual passion and the deepest religious faith. Poor guy, Jackie must have thought. His girlfriend probably wore an overcoat to bed. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93It\'92s better than the other systems we see,\'94 interjected Clive. \'93Sure, some- times they get lucky. Just like when a lamb goes to the slaughter, the lamb might kill the butcher. But I\'92ll always bet on the butcher.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley, remembering the butcher story from Barney\'92s, thought it must have been a line taught at the Pit Boss Institute of Technology. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93We\'92re vegetarians,\'94 chimed in Vivian, she too remembering the episode at Barney\'92s. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93You meet all kinds,\'94 said Jackie, trying to lighten up the table. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93There was a guy from Illinois,\'94 recalled Clive. \'93Called himself the Stargazer. Had some kind of magic toy slot machine. He rolled it before every hand. If it came up cherries, he bet. If not, he laid out. And darned it but he usually seemed to bet right.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Heresy!\'94 exclaimed Allan. \'93I put my trust in the Lord, not in pagan instru- ments or the devil\'92s toys.\'94 Oakley thought that Allan was putting it on a bit too much. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93A real character,\'94 continued Clive. \'93He used to order drinks, liqueurs, the expensive stuff, and never drink \'91em. Had a bottle hidden behind one of the slot machines. He\'92d leave the table and pour the booze into the bottle.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Talk about coming for the free drinks,\'94 said Oakley. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Clive pointed to the cocktail glasses on the table. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Sort of like you kids, huh? It\'92s fine. But maybe you should learn how to play blackjack a little better.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93This is exactly how I always play,\'94 said Oakley. \'93Same strategy. I stand at twelve or above so I never go over twenty-one.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Me, too,\'94 added Allan. \'93I always count on the Lord.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley would later tell me about the Stargazer. Apparently he had a lot in common with him, and they by some quirk of fate, became acquainted through mutual friends in the UCLA math department. The Stargazer was a graduate student in astronomy from Berkeley, although Vivian said she had never met him. He apparently had been one of the founders of the University of California Jazz Ensembles, the group for which Vivian was a vocalist. You recall she met Oakley at the Pacific Coast Collegiate Jazz. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He had played very low stakes poker games with his twin brother and bud- dies during high school, and a little blackjack. In preparation for the first trip of the band to the Reno Jazz Festival, under the direction of David Tucker, who was also the director under whom Vivian sang, he read a book on black- jack by Oswald Jacoby, the bridge maven that he had bought at the famous Moe\'92s Bookstore on Telegraph Avenue. To prepare, he and his girlfriend, Diane Dring, whose mother had been a vocalist herself, practiced in the din of the Bear\'92s Lair snack room on the Berkeley campus. Having never been to an actual casino, he wanted to simulate its noise and distractions. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The Stargazer was sufficiently successful on that Reno Jazz Festival trip that he would make monthly trips to Reno for the rest of his graduate student days at Berkeley. He\'92d leave on a Sunday afternoon, when the other casino play- ers would be returning to the Bay Area, and stay through Tuesday afternoon. The chartered bus that the residents of Chinatown would take to Reno and back, the \'93chicken soup express,\'94 so named because the Chinese would bring containers of chicken soup with them from San Francisco for the 3 1/4 hour trip, would be packing up to leave as the Stargazer arrived. He always stayed at a small hotel, the El Cortez, that in any other city would have been a brothel or vagrant\'92s destination. It\'92s still there in Reno, west of Virginia Street, a small hotel, with a red neon sign, and single elevator right there in the small, do- not-tell, front lobby. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Despite betting low amounts, the Stargazer\'92s frequent visits began to draw attention. After all, even if you win only $50 at a shift, that\'92s $100 that the casino has to make up to earn their $50. So, as Oakley told me, the Stargazer came up with booze as a camouflage technique. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 If you\'92re a serious player, you should never drink while playing. Among other things, it interferes with your rational decision making. The Stargazer, who Oakley told me was also an accomplished actor, played the lush to hide 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 14 Stargazing 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 129 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 130 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 being a systems player. Before sitting at a table, he would dab some booze on his neck, face, and hands so he would emit that telltale fragrance. This, Oakley recalled, was exactly one of the camouflage techniques suggested by Manny during the Brookline visit. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Immediately upon sitting down, the Stargazer would order a drink, straight, no rocks, and if it didn\'92t arrive quickly he\'92d make a small fuss. He didn\'92t order the usual Scotch or bourbon, but the expensive liqueurs, amaretto, kah- lua, and cr\'e8me de menthe. He\'92d pretend to take a hit from time to time but wouldn\'92t really drink. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 When his session was over, he would stumble slightly on leaving the table saying, \'93Time to try the slots.\'94 Even that statement was a camouflage, imply- ing he was a gambler. No system exists at the slot machines. He would walk over to the rows of slot machines and pour whatever drink he had into a bottle he had hidden between a couple of the slot machines. Judging from Clive the pit boss\'92 comments, the casinos eventually caught on to the hidden bottle trick. Nonetheless, as you\'92ll see, Oakley would learn from the Stargazer. He would later develop and use camouflage techniques of his own. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Here, at Harold\'92s Club, they were already using some. They were pretend- ing to be mooching drinks, again as Manny had suggested, and playing based on faith and superstition, in Allan\'92s case, and playing using na\'efve systems, the \'93do not bust\'94 strategy, in Oakley\'92s case. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 *** 
\fs24 \

\fs32 It was evening. Oakley, Vivian, Allan, Joan, Julian, and Nancy had enjoyed a nice dinner at the Riverside Hotel, later to be known as Jessie Beck\'92s Riverside. To get there from the main casino strip on Virginia Street you had to cross what everyone referred to as the river. It was, by name, the Truckee River, although in Reno it was no more than a large, lazy stream rather than a broad expanse reminiscent of the great American rivers or even the rushing Truckee further up the mountains near its source. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 They sat down on a couple of benches facing the river and listened to the sounds of the reclaimed desert night. Oakley had been thinking about the entire escapade, learning a system, trying out the system, proving the skeptics wrong. He was wondering about the place of such ventures in the fabric of one\'92s life. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He had pointed out Venus in the sky, only a month past eastern elongation, making it startlingly bright in the early evening hours, and his friends were enthralled by it and the multitude of other stars visible in the non-polluted skies over Reno. One day, he had told Vivian, he wanted to build an astro- nomical observatory to indulge his interest in that subject. Some birds scam- pered along the river edge, looking for seeds fallen from the trees growing on 
\fs24 \

\fs32 the river bank. Joan noted that they were probably vegetarians and that the worms there were safe. They watched the birds. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93We\'92re born to die,\'94 Oakley said, breaking the silence. \'93You start to die when you watch those you love die. That\'92s what \'91over the hill\'92 means. What hill? You\'92re over the hill when those you love have died.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Whoa,\'94 exclaimed Allan. \'93Where did that come from, Ed?\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93That sounds like a Jewish piece of wisdom,\'94 noted Julian. \'93We\'92re born to die.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93You\'92re exactly right, Julian,\'94 said Vivian. \'93I learned the phrase from my parents, Abe and Adele. And this guy learned it from me.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I don\'92t care if it\'92s your dog or your parakeet or your parents,\'94 continued Oakley. That\'92s when the sense of humor starts to go and you stop caring about life. You let yourself go physically and don\'92t eat well. You\'92re getting ready to die.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Well,\'94 said Vivian, \'93I hope most people wait a few decades after those events have hit their lives.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93All of life is full of highs and lows,\'94 said Nancy. \'93My uncle Eliezer Moshe always says, \'91As night follows day, 
\f2\i tsouris 
\f1\i0 follows 
\f2\i nachas
\f1\i0 ,\'92 misery and joy.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93So why do we screw?\'94 asked Allan.\uc0\u8232 \'93Where\'92d that come from?\'94 Vivian said, repeating Allan\'92s comment. \'93That\'92s not the question,\'94 said Oakley. \'93That\'92s the wrong question. The 
\fs24 \

\fs32 question is why does it feel so good to screw?\'94

\fs32 \cf3 Julian and Nancy excused themselves, but the others returned to Harold\'92s Club to try out the complete point count system. Manny, unlike in the diver- sionary afternoon session, was intent on watching. He, with Oakley, Vivian, Allan, and Joan, walked north across the bridge. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Now with serious gambling time approaching, Oakley hoped that he would face no difficulty from the pit boss, that he would not be taken as a serious threat to Harold\'92s profits. That was the point of the gambling during the afternoon. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Reno was a good place to try out Julian and Oakley\'92s system. Las Vegas is for vacationers from all over the world, those who are willing to spend a lot of money on lavish rooms, dinners, shows, and gambling, or just to be able to say they were going to \'93Vegas,\'94 implying they were adventurous and oblivious to danger, a modern swashbuckler. South Lake Tahoe draws people from the Bay Area for its skiing, scenic woods and lakes, and romance, with gambling a side attraction. South Lake Tahoe is, strictly speaking, a vacation rather than gambling destination. I think I noted that before. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Reno, though, is a gambler\'92s town. In Las Vegas, unless you\'92re playing downtown, you need a car to go from casino to casino. In Reno, they are side to side on Virginia Street, smaller venues like the Money Tree, Silver Spur, and Primadonna, as well as the big name operators such as Harrah\'92s, Harold\'92s Club, Fitzgerald\'92s, the Cal Neva, the Mapes Hotel and, across the river, the Riverside Hotel. Virginia Street is almost congested, like the stores on a strip mall. It reminded Oakley of the portside merchant stalls in Madras, India, where he had given a paper at the renowned Presidency College at a

\fs32 \cf3 symposium to honor the great astrophysicist Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar, a 1930 graduate of that university who would win the Nobel Prize for his work in developing the theory of stellar structure and evolution. Each such stall shared a wall with its neighbors, with a single merchant seated at a chair at its front. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Before returning to Harold\'92s Club, Oakley and Allan decided to warm up at a couple of the smaller casinos. Call them practice sessions. At the small Money Tree, having only two tables, Oakley bet $10 to $100 depending on the value of the high-low index, and within minutes won about $250. He stayed for several more hours and won another $400 or so. Manny, Vivian, and Joan watched. A trip for fun with your girlfriends and, perhaps, your father, makes for excellent camouflage. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Then they walked over to the Silver Spur, for their second and final warm- up. Feeling more at ease with the system, Oakley\'92s betting now ranged from $25 to $250. In fifteen minutes, he rang up another $500 in winnings. Allan was playing the basic strategy at the same table, having not studied the com- plete point count. That these friends did not make the same strategy decisions provided another effective camouflage. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Nonetheless, Oakley\'92s winnings led the dealer to lean forward over the table and press a concealed button on the floor with her foot, a call to casino management. Two managers appeared, exchanged greetings with Oakley and his friends, and then instructed the dealer to shuffle the deck between 12 and 15 cards from the end. Casinos had learned, over the previous decade, that some players would wait until near the end of the deck and then jump their bets from $1 to as much as $500. Not knowing what the players were doing, but realizing they were cashing in, the dealers would be instructed to shuffle the deck five to ten cards from the end. Management had decided that Oakley was one such player. Oakley would later take great advantage of what he would call \'93end play.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Yet the ensuing decks were favorable, with positive high-low indices, and Oakley continued to win. Management returned and told the dealer to shuffle 25 cards from the end. Allan now decided to lay out, letting Oakley play by himself. The decks continued to favor Oakley, and he continued to win. Management returned again, and told the dealer to shuffle 42 cards from the end of the deck, after only two hands had been dealt. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley continued to play for another twenty minutes or so. Despite the deck becoming unfavorable, the house possessing some unfavorable table rules, and the early shuffling, Oakley was able to win another $80. With win- nings of nearly $2000, it seemed pointless to continue at the truncated-deck addicted Silver Spur.

\fs32 \cf3 With hours of practice behind him, Oakley and his entourage of hardened gamblers moved on to Harold\'92s Club for the kill. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley purchased $2000 in chips from the cashier and then he and Allan sat at a blackjack table set against the wall in what could best be described as a corridor between two large gambling rooms. They hoped that they could have the table to themselves, gamblers preferring the glitz of the larger rooms. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 During the next couple of hours, much of the time Oakley and Allan did have the fortune of having no other players at the table. Yet Oakley had the misfortune, to use an inappropriate term, of losing steadily. The point count had frequently been negative, leading him to usually bet his minimum $25. Yet, he was down nearly $1700, which nearly wiped out all of his earnings at the Money Tree and Silver Spur. He was, understandably, discouraged. He fought off any thoughts that although the complete point count system was sure to win in the long run, perhaps, in the more realistic short run, streaks of bad luck would make the system non-viable. Perhaps that book that he and Julian had planned on writing would not be written. Maybe he should just publish his dissertation, the traditional manner of advancing one\'92s nascent academic career. It would certainly please Vivian. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan was taking frequent breaks, letting Oakley play heads-up against the dealer. When he did play, he was also losing, but, not playing Oakley\'92s stakes, the amount was only a hundred dollars or so. Then things changed. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley had laid down a bet of three hundred dollars in $25 chips. Allan, playing to Oakley\'92s left, had bet $20, four $5 chips. Their two cards were face down. Joan, Vivian, and Manny stood behind them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Jo, the dealer, had an up-card of the six of Clubs. The only other witness was Elroy, the pit boss, who stood behind Jo watching the action. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Excluding the burned card, which had not been viewed and which was therefore, according to Oakley\'92s complete point count system, not to be included in the tallies, 16 cards had been played out of a newly shuffled deck. The point count before the hand he now held had been +9. The high-low index had therefore been +9/36 \'d7 100, or +25, calling for a bet of 12 units of Oakley\'92s minimum bet of $25, or $300. This was the table maximum at some Reno casinos, a large bet, but not at Harold\'92s, where the table maximum was $500. Betting the table maximum would certainly have attracted significant management attention. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley held a pair of eights, the eight of Clubs and the eight of Diamonds, each of point value zero. Jo\'92s up-card of six had a point value of +1. The updated point count, which Oakley referred to as the \'93running count,\'94 was therefore now +10, and the high-low index was +10/33 \'d7 100, or about +30. In the simple point count system the point count was only updated after 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 \cf3 144 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 all the cards had been played, as a guide to the bet for the next hand. In the complete point count system, the running count, obtained by adjusting the point count as every card was dealt and seen, was kept to guide the play of the hand in progress. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley flipped over his two cards. He separated them, indicating he was going to split the pair. This is a standard bet for anyone with the minimum knowledge of blackjack, sixteen being a lousy hand. Oakley didn\'92t have enough chips on the table to cover the entire bet, but when you split a pair blackjack rules at the vast majority of casinos require you to match the origi- nal bet, and Oakley wanted to do so. He was too involved in the hand to even consider playing dumb. He reached into his pocket and pulled out his wallet. He removed three $100 bills and placed them on the table next to the eight of Diamonds. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Money plays,\'94 announced Jo. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 She dealt the three of Clubs next to the eight of Diamonds and the ten of Hearts next to the eight of Clubs. With point values of +1 and \'961, these cards canceled each other out. The high-low index was now +10/31 \'d7 100, or about +33. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley pulled out another three $100 bills from his wallet and placed them next to the eight of Diamonds. Even the basic strategy directed, as most all players know, that you double down with a hard total of 11 against the dealer\'92s up-card of 6. The complete point count tables that he and Julian had developed said to double down when the high-low index exceeded \uc0\u8722 35. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Such an exceedingly large negative index would result from, for example, a point count of \uc0\u8722 7 with only twenty cards remaining in the deck to be played, \'967/20 \'d7 100 = \'9635. That meant, of the twenty remaining cards, at least seven, the exact number depending on the number of ten-value cards or Aces also remaining, would need to have point values of +1, the 2\'92s through 6\'92s. If, for example, four ten-value cards or Aces were present, then the deck would have to have eleven of these low-value cards, the rest being the sevens, eights, and nines, with zero point value. Other than such unfavorable situations, the complete point count system directs the player to double down with a hard 11 against the dealer\'92s up-card of 6. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Double.\'94 Perhaps Oakley was getting carried away. The term for doubling your bet is \'93double-down.\'94 One needs a fair amount of experience to feel comfortable using the abbreviated term, and the whole point of the diversion in the afternoon was to convince the casino that he was not an experienced player. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Vivian was alarmed by the amount of money Oakley had wagered. \'93$900, Edward,\'94 she advised.

\fs32 nine of Clubs and the seven of Diamonds. When Allan had called for a card, the high-low index had been +10/30 \'d7 100, or still +33, Oakley calculated. \fs24 \

\fs32 a smirk on his face. Vivian stared at Oakley, shaking with silent laughter. He had just pocketed $900, cash. That was more than his beginning $7000 annual salary would generate in more than a month and a half. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 They weren\'92t aware of the spy cameras in the ceiling peering at them. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The deck was still \'93hot,\'94 the high-low index being +10/28 \'d7 100, or about +35, and, having established this betting pattern, Oakley continued to place bets larger than the minimum. In the one deck he made up his $1700 in losses at Harold\'92s Club. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 Oakley knew that winning that amount of money in one deck, let alone one hand, wouldn\'92t be duplicated soon and accordingly decided to take a break. He had experienced what he would experience many times over, mod- erately heavy losing streaks, followed by dazzlingly brilliant streaks of large winning hands. This had taught him an important lesson. He could not allow himself to be discouraged by the losses to the extent that he lost faith in the system and either lost discipline and stopped obeying its directives or, even more unconscionably, abandoned playing. In addition, suddenly decreasing his bets to the minimum would tip off the casino, at least Elroy, that he might have a viable system. It was time to take a break. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 He and Allan stood up from the table and, with Vivian, Joan, and Manny, they left the table and walked out of the corridor into Harold\'92s lobby. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93My linear operator!\'94 exclaimed Vivian, giving her suddenly wealthy hus- band a hug. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Not bad for five or ten minutes work,\'94 added Allan. \'93A few more hands like that and they\'92ll have to give you tenure,\'94 he joked. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah, but now you gotta be careful,\'94 said Manny, stating the obvious. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Yeah,\'94 agreed Oakley. That kind of action is exactly what draws casino attention. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93Look, I think I better not go on the rounds with you no more,\'94 advised Manny. \'93They know my face. Right now you\'92re just a kid looking for a lounge act. I\'92ll just wait outside.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 \'93I think that\'92s right,\'94 agreed Oakley.\uc0\u8232 \'93By the way,\'94 Joan wondered, \'93anyone seen Julian and Nancy?\'94\u8232 \'93Probably taking a dancing lesson,\'94 suggested Manny, smiling. \'93Horizontal 
\fs24 \

\fs32 mambo or something.\'94\uc0\u8232 Vivian had seen Manny trying to pull Nancy toward him when they were 
\fs24 \

\fs32 dancing in the Pair-o\'92-Dice Lounge. She didn\'92t appreciate the sexual reference and glared at Manny. 
\fs24 \

\fs24 \

\fs32 Oakley, Vivian, and Joan decided to get a celebratory snack, but Allan, although he wasn\'92t playing the point count system, wanted to continue play- ing. He had perhaps gotten the bug. He had, in fact, gotten the bug to such an extent that he would himself eventually author a book on gambling. Oakley told him to avoid Harold\'92s, so Allan went down the street to the Cal Neva. After about an hour they met in Oakley\'92s room at the Mapes Hotel. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan had been playing blackjack and had an interesting tale to tell his friends. The pit boss and dealer were discussing some guy who had carried what they called a magic slot machine. He always wore a white turtleneck with a gold chain around his neck, which he said was for \'93good luck.\'94 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 15 Toy Slot Machines and Other Diversions 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 147 
\f1\b0\fs24 \

\fs32 \cf3 In any case, Allan\'92s eavesdropping allowed him to fill in the story of the guy with the turtleneck and gold chain. It seems he had gotten a little plastic toy slot machine as a souvenir welcome gift at the Westward Ho motel in Las Vegas years earlier. The toy was actually operational with a functional handle and all the fruit symbols on three separate wheels. Apparently, he would spin the toy slot machine handle and show the result to everyone at the table. If the three columns of fruit came up all cherries he would bet. Otherwise, he would not. The guy, who liked to be called Goose, would win an uncanny number of hands when he bet. When he lay out, the other players would more often than not lose. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Goose, according to Allan\'92s understanding, quickly attained cult status. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 At the Four Queens downtown a couple years earlier, Goose almost lost the toy slot. For four straight deals everyone lost, but Goose had lain out. On the next hand, Goose pulled the slot machine handle, showed it was all cher- ries, laid down a $100 bill and got blackjack. The guy sitting next to him got enraged and grabbed for the toy slot, saying, \'93Let me see that slot machine.\'94 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Generally, if you sit you have to play. If you don\'92t want to bet, protocol states that you should leave the table and give up your seat to someone else. The dealers usually enforce this, though gently. Here, though, was a guy with a good luck gold chain and a seemingly cosmically connected magical toy, and the dealers and pit bosses were as intrigued by it as much as anyone else and they let him play. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Later, walking across Virginia Street, Allan related that he saw a guy wear- ing a white turtleneck with a gold chain around his neck. On a whim, he asked the guy if his name was Goose. The man was surprised at this question, according to Allan, but admitted he was the same. Goose wanted to know if Allan worked for one of the casinos, and Allan told him that he was a math- ematician and that he and another mathematician were testing out a system for blackjack. This apparently opened the figurative floodgates of verbiage, because Goose admitted that he was an electrical engineer, with a bachelors\'92 and masters\'92 degree in the difficult VI-2 honors program from, of all places, MIT, and that he, too, had a system. It called for lying out when the deck favored the house and betting heavily when it favored the player. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan asked him about the magic slot machine. He related, with some enthusiasm, as if years had gone by without his being able to share the infor- mation, that he had discovered while playing with the slot machine that the slot machine was defective. If he pressed in slightly on the two sides of the toy slot with his thumb and index finger, it would come up a winning three cherries. Without that pressure, the slot would come up other, random, non- winning fruity combinations. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b\fs26\fsmilli13333 \cf3 148 
\fs24 Never Split Tens 
\f1\b0 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 Just like Allan and Oakley were camouflaging their play by pretending to be musicians, as Manny had suggested, Goose was camouflaging his play. He would sit at the table and pull the lever. When the deck was cold, he wouldn\'92t press in the sides, random fruit would appear, he\'92d display it to the dealer and the other players, and would not bet. Goose had a perfectly rational excuse. \'93It\'92s bad luck unless the cherries come up,\'94 he would say. When the deck was hot, Goose would pull the lever, push in the sides of the magic slot machine, and display for all to see that the machine god had decreed that he lay down a bet. He would nearly always win when the magic slot machine came up cher- ries, and the other players would usually lose when it did not. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan remembered that Clive, the pit boss at Harold\'92s Club, had men- tioned that the gambler who called himself the Stargazer used a similar device. On mentioning this to Goose, Goose laughed and admitted that the Stargazer was in fact his twin brother and that a couple years earlier they had traveled through Nevada, gambling together in Reno, South Lake Tahoe, Vegas, and Carson City. Goose took the magic slot machine with him on every gambling trip. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Allan\'92s narrative, in fact, emboldened Oakley. Here was a guy, by all evi- dence, genuine, who had a system enabling him to distinguish cold from hot decks. It was essentially what he and Julian Braun had developed. He would have loved to have met Goose and to have compared systems, but that was the last time any of them encountered him. Perhaps Goose, on reconsidera- tion, had thought Allan was in fact lying and was indeed a casino employee, and that it was best to leave town before either his photograph was taken for dissemination, which would ruin his game for the future, or he was barred or even arrested for breathing too much air or walking on the wrong side of the street. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 *** 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Before the proliferation of relatively low cost television monitors and switching circuits driven by computer software, the tables and slot machines were monitored by personnel in the rooms in the ceiling above the casino floor, the security office. They\'92d be seated in front of closed-circuit television monitors, each person monitoring a few screens. The images were captured by security cameras sitting behind one-way mirrors in the ceilings of casi- nos. Some referred to them as eyes in the sky. Today, the security rooms can be located anywhere in the building, wiring being able to carry the signals anywhere. 
\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\f0\b \cf3 15 Toy Slot Machines and Other Diversions 
\fs26\fsmilli13333 149 
\f1\b0\fs24 \
\pard\pardeftab720\sa240\partightenfactor0

\fs32 \cf3 These surveillance systems and the monitoring personnel paid for themselves many times over. They would look for cheaters, both players and dealers in cahoots with players. The personnel, most of whom were former dealers and perhaps former pit bosses, knew the kinds of signals between dealers and players used. They particularly paid attention to high rollers. A gambler betting $500 or more a hand could pick up thousands of dollars on a hot streak, and the casino wanted to make sure that in such circumstances only luck was involved. They\'92d look for the player marking cards, either by bending them or putting foreign substances on their backs. They\'92d look for players repeatedly taking hits when they should have stood or standing when they should have taken a card, both indicating that the dealer was peaking at the next card to be dealt and providing the drawing or standing advice to the player. It was a continual game of spy versus spy, the cheating players and the casino trying to stay abreast of each other\'92s techniques. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Sometimes, if a player had won some significant money, the dealer would go upstairs and sit and watch after his shift change. If the player reappeared on another shift, and that dealer was in the casino, he may be called to go upstairs and observe again. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Slot machine players would also be observed. Although historically mechan- ical devices, more and more were electronic, and these could potentially face tampering by a sophisticated cheat. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 Generally, however, life in the sky couldn\'92t be more boring. Most players are unsophisticated. This is particularly true in Reno, which lacks the glitz and high rollers of Las Vegas and the romance and well-heeled skiing clientele of South Lake Tahoe. Reno attracts the unfortunate fringe, who can stay in cheap rooming houses or hotels while trying to make a few bucks in the casi- nos. Except for the occasional spilled drink or cigarette burn on the green felt, they create little problems in the casinos. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The security personnel would look at the cameras, finding nothing, and munch on doughnuts and pretzels, with a sip of soft drink or coffee. Because of the nature of their work, and its potential importance, booze was not allowed in the security rooms. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 If something out of the ordinary were occurring, the security man would call down to the pit using phones sitting on tables next to the closed-circuit television monitors. Today something out of the ordinary was occurring. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 A middle-aged man sat before a couple of the monitors, a blank stare on his face. He munched on a cinnamon roll. He picked up the phone and dialed. 
\fs24 \

\fs32 The phone on Elroy\'92s table rang. Elroy reluctantly left the dealer with whom he was chatting, and picked up the phone.
